No medical man advertising in these colonies has medical

ONLY LEGALLY QUALIFIED DOCTOR

No diseases cause so much misery and distress as those
included in my speciality.
All Men Young and Old,
Suffering from the results of early follies or ex-
cesses, indigestion, foul mouth, costiveness, palpi-

NERVOUS DEBILITY, should lose no time in

Each steamer carries a duly qualified Surgeon.

Inman, White Star, and other Companies.

SEND FOR NEW ILLUSTRATED CATALOGUE.
The Co-Operative
SYSTEM, the nearest approach to Socialism that can he achieved under the
present conditions of life, has met with phenomenal success wherever it has
been applied to any object. In England, even the Co-operative Societies
benefit some millions of persons—their membership exceeding a million and
a-half, and hold invested capital to the amount of eleven millions of money.
This success is owing to the fact that the investor sees not only a fair com-
mercial return on his investment, but a proprietary division of profits, which
he would not receive from any other organisation. It has been left to Aus-
tralia to apply this communistic’ principle to
Irrigation
and the CO-OPERATIVE IRRIGATION and MERCANTILE SOCIETY
of .AUSTRALLA., Ltd., have already secured three irrigation colonies in
Victoria, which are partially settled and planted, and are negotiating for the
purchase of a tract of magnificent irrigation country in the West. Nego-
tiations are also pending for a Government concession of Murray land, and
there are now
Colonies
half-populated, awaiting the small Capitalist, the employed ARTISAN, or
the man whose sole CAPITAL is his LABOUR.

The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in tne
“ CoiTcspondcncc" column all contributions submitted —■
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,

tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns- in

Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.

(he address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But tve
desire those sending MSS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for the preservation or transmission
of such MSS.
NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty Shillings
sterling—which you may pay by Post Office Order, One
Pound Note, or Stamps of any Australian colony—you
can have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week
for a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with idiom we do not wish
to interfere.
SxVTURDAY, SEPTEMBER 26, 1891.
Roses and Asafoetida.
“You may break, you may shatter, the vase,
if you will, but the odour of roses will cling
to it still.” Thus sings a poet. That other
odoriferous material which is named in the
heading of this article appears not to have
been fraught with suggestion to the poetic
imagination. Lions have ramped and snakes
have crawled in many a fluent verse ; but no
one has adventured even so much as a
sonnet on the skunk. And yet it would
seem to be, in some sense, apropos of the
latter animal that Queen Victoria has been
pleased to elevate to the peerage of Great
Britain the relict of the Grand Old Man of
the Dominion of Canada, Sir John Mac-
donald, Of course we take no interest in
the lady thus ennobled. What we enjoy is
our own reflections respecting the cause of
her elevation —viz., the distinguished ser-
vices of her late spouse. Sir John Mac-
donald was undoubtedly a very remarkable
man. The gigantic dimensions of his politi-
cal stature rendered him visible even at the
distance which separates Australia from
Canada, long before that enthusiastic apostle
of mud and holiness, Mr. Stead, had a fit of
printed hysterics on merely contemplating
the grandeur of the Canadian statesman.
It is perhaps a testimony to the purely
inspirational origin of Mr. Stead’s convul-
sions, that in the course of the same fit
he frothed at the mouth about Sir John
Macdonald, Sir Henry Parkes, and Mr.
Cecil Rhodes. At the same time that asso-
ciation of individuals may possibly be re-
garded as somewhat derogatory by any one
or any two of the triplet so grouped. Still,
in Mr. Stead’s very next severe seizure the
spirit moved him to bear testimony to the
pure and lofty motives which actuated the
Prince of Wales in maintaining, as part of his
travelling equipment, a gambling-kit, and in
cracking the crib of a serious family with
those tools, and so the trio are in good
society.
# * *
Respecting Mr. Cecil Rhodes, the Cape
Premier, not much is yet known. The
salient features in the non-teetotal career of
that great man, so far, are confined to the cir-
cumstances that his first appearance in South
African society was as a street-huckster in
Capetown, that ho joined the Diamond-fields
rush, struck it rich, became monstrously
wealthy, bought himself into public life,
evinced possession of masterful talent and
inordinate ambition, and seems inclined and
qualified to checkmate the Boer aspirations.
Mr. Rhodes, however, is comparatively a
young man, and only time can show whether
lie is reasonably entitled to figure in the
same category with Macdonald and Parkes.
The flavour of Mr. Cecil Rhodes is not as
yet very powerful or clearly defined. Res-
pecting Parkes, who emits his sweet-
ness in our immediate vicinity, there can, of
course, be no mistake. His moral odour
speaks for itself. And as for Sir John
Macdonald, he is dead, but his reputation
certainly predeceased him. That is to say, if
the term “reputation” be employed in a
wholesome sense. For many years past all
who knew him have been conscious that an
odour of corruption diffused itself from the
decayed matter which the creatures of the
Canadian statesman called lbs public charac-
ter. The man was understood to be one of
consummate tactical skill in politics, and
totally unscrupulous as to the methods by
which he maintained his supremacy. The
broad outlines of his qualities were probably
seen more justly by persons at a distance
than by those nearer to him. It is truly
remarkable how accurately the really pro-
minent characteristics of a notable public
man come to be understood where there
exist no interested reasons for distorting
them.
* # #
The great ability of the deceased Canadian
statesman is evidenced, in the first place, by
the extraordinary collapse of the entire
political system which he created and main-
tained while alive, and which died along
with him. All through his latter years it
was generally understood that his power was
founded on and sustained by the most
shameless corruption. But so expert had
“Old To-morrow” become in staving off
exposure, that so long as he lived he was
able to set his opponents at defiance, and
attitudinise as not merely an able but an
upright administrator. In this style of
posturing he was powerfully assisted by his
constant assumption of Imperialistic zeal,
and of personal loyalty to the throne. To
cast aspersions on the methods and character
of the one colonial statesman whom the
Sovereign distinguished above all others was
to fly in the face of Majesty, and asperse
alike the sagacity and the integrity of the
Queen’s advisers, and of the Queen herself.
A swarm of highly-placed Ministeis, of
leading officials, of social personages, of
wealthy monopolists and influential em-
ployers of labour, of favoured or subsidised
journalists, and of aspirants after office,
vehemently asserted the absolute impecca-
bility of their revered ringleader. He lived
by corruption and hired grovel, and when
lie died his character fell to pieces on the
instant.
* * •
The first shock was dealt by the mere
publication of the dead statesman’s will.
The simple circumstance that Sir John
Macdonald had in possession certain shares
in the Canadian trans-continental railway
spread confusion and consternation through-
out the Dominion. It was not merely
because his wealth proved to be more con-
siderable than could bo reconciled with his
pursuits and apparent transactions and
opportunities ; the particular nature of these
securities gave sinister significance to the
fact that they were in his possession
at all. They had been kept a secret
from his contemporaries, as a wealthy
financier might have kept forged bonds or
stolen scrip. Under no conceivable circum-
stances could they have come honestly into
his hands, and how they might have become
his by corruption was so obvious that no one
but a simpleton could overlook the proba-
bilities.
* * *
After some desperate efforts to explain
away the unexplainable, recourse was had to
silence. The only device available in the
case of an irremediable exposure was adopted.
The scandal was “hushed up.” Immense
influence must have been exerted to
achieve this end. The press purist,
Stead, found his spirit moved to cast
a halo of eulogy around the repu-
tations of Sir John Macdonald, Sir
Henry Parkes, and Mr. Cecil Rhodes
simultaneously with the outcropping in
Canada, in Australia, and in the Cape
Colony of indications that a comprehensive
Imperialistic conspiracy to draw tighter
“ tlxe golden bonds” was in active opera-
tion. This was immediately succeeded by
an almost reckless attempt to whitewash the
besmirched reputation of the Prince of
Wales. The source and fountain-head of
influence is not difficult to discover by these
indications. The “ hushing-up ” process in
relation to Sir John Macdonald’s plunder
might have succeeded had he still lived.
But death having stilled his master hand and
ingenious brain, the gang of public robbers
of which he had been the patron and pro-
tector were left without a leader. Dis-
organisation and dismay assailed them.
They were very corrupt, like their dead
chief, but not, like him, as dexterous as they
were conscienceless. One after another
they succumb under the stress of investiga-
tion, until at length a scare has set in, and
their once imposing attitude has been aban-
doned, and is supplanted by a regular
satire qui pent. Government contractors,
having no longer the influence of the once
omnipotent Premier to shield them, endea-
vour to anticipate compromising disclosures
by voluntary informations, probably told in
the way least damaging to themselves.
Fearful that if they persist in their long-
sus'ained silence, they will be condemned as
accomplices, they solicit commiseration as
the victims of political and official black-
mailers. A State Premier’s financial secre-
tary absconds almost at the same time as a
member of the Legislature bolts. The latter
had refused to answer interrogatories, alleging
the singular plea that he could not do so
without implicating his political associates.
The great departments of State are dis-
covered to have been hot-beds of corruption
and embezzlement. Atrocious consequences
these of the decease of an alleged Grand Old
Man !
* # *
This Canadian catastrophe is fruitful of
premonitions for communities similarly cir-
cumstanced. Wherever there exists a
G.0.M., around ivliosc career hangs an
odour of corruption, there similar disclosures
may be expected when any event removes
the chief actor from the scene without
chance of restoration. The lesson should
be regarded as peculiarly pregnant of warn-
ings, in any community where a powerful
leader scarcely manages to repress present
revolt, even while at • the summit of his
influence. If there should ever exist, for
example, a province, the political head of
which has a reputation which stinks in the
nostrils of his neighbours, and is only en-
dured by exertion of the most charitable
suspension of judgment and exceptional
laxity of principle among bis own fellow-
citizens, there a catastrophe similar to that
which has occurred in Canada may be
reasonably apprelundcd. And when such a
man is made the mark of the most flagrant
aspersions, assailing alike his morals, his
commercial probity and bis political inte-
grity, and yet fails to put l lie law into opera-
tion to silence and punish his detractors,
some frightful scandal must be looked for as
inevitable the moment the weight of his
personal influence's removed. Men of such
a stamp resemble those fabled wretches who
sold their souls to the Devil for as urance of a
period of brilliant prosperity. They gain the
brilliance and the prosperity only to find
that these are Dead Sea fruit, fa»r to out-
ward view, but to the consumer full of
nought but bitterness and ashes. The end
of it all is constantly present before their
minds. They contemplate with horror and
despair the day or the night when their
infernal master will claim them, and leave
behind the tokens of their bargain in a
blackened and blasted reputation. Vainly
they strive and struggle by every imaginable
device to defeat the contract. They hire
scribes to prepare in advance of the
catastrophe, eulogies to serve for biographies.
They write their own memoirs in blazons of
virtue and worth. They masquerade as
saints, with the object that when they are
gone, people may be slow to recognise that
they were damned in advance.
** • #
The evil such men do “ lives after them,”
while the little go >d they may have accom-
plished “is buried with their bones.” A
political leader, such as has been portrayed,
corrupts an entire community, and if
be live long enough debases more than
one generation. As he sways the
destinies of a people, and exercises the
power to make or mar, in some degree,
the fortunes of individuals, it is soon recog-
nised that uprightness and loftiness of prin-
ciple are no guarantees of worldly prosperity.
The unscrupulousschemer finds theback-doors
which lead, over gutters and sewers, to the
place where fat contracts and comfortable
billets are arranged, always ajar to him. The
ambitious youth, aflame for a career of dis-
tinction, the acquisitive anhungered-after
aTliicncc, perceive that virtuous squeamish-
noss will more likely mar than mend them,
’i lie places of pride, the seats of power, the
.si! utions of emolumentare allotted preferen-
tially to accomplices, confederates, tools,
and sycophants, who have either no preju-
dices against corruption to overcome, or who
are too weak to resist the influence of
example, or too worldly-wise to refrain from
conformity with iniquity. The poison
extends through all conditions of men. The
foreman and the confidential clerk learn how
it is that this employer never fails to secure
profitable jobs, while that has his tenders
set aside. Neither sex escapes the infection.
The very school-girls, whose education
and up-bringing the State has made its
peculiar care, may be taught by example
thrust upon their notice, that it is more
advantageous to play the harlot to an in-
fluential debaucher than to seek haj piness in
virtue and a marriage in their own condition
of life. Thus the venom engendered by one
vicious being penetrates every stratum of
society, and ravages its inmost tissues.
Generations have to pass away before the
foul corruption is fairly expelled. But per-
chance we declaim in vain.
An Established. Church for Australia.
On a recent Sunday, at St. James’ Church,
Sydney, a lecture was given by the incumbent,
the Rev. 11. Latimer Jackson, on “Some
arguments in favour of an Established
Church.” It is quite a new departure for
The Bulletin to devote more than a short
paragraph to clerical vagaries, but Mr. Jack-
son’s lecture so felicitously showed the width
of the chasm separating the lay from the
clerical mind—so aptly illustrated the fact that
the ordinary clergyman is entirely out of touch
with the community which surrounds and sup-
ports him—that the discourse, if but for its
bizarrerie, deserves something more than a
brief analysis. For reasons that are unfor-
tunately but too apparent the clergy have been
described as “ the third sex.” The designation,
if a little rude, cannot in fairness be con-
sidered undeserved. In their teachings, and
sometimes in their conduct, the most note-
worthy feature is the almost entire absence of
that virility which characterises the vigorous
masculine intellect, as well as of the delicacy and
originality to be found in the refined female in-
telligence. It is through this cause, among many
others tending in the same direction, that their
views in these days have came to be regarded
cither with good-natured toleration or with
undisguised indifference, not to say contempt.
Mr. Jackson began by inviting bis bearers to
consider bow important it was that true religi 011
“ should make headway in this young country ”
Conceding this, the question naturally suggests
itself: “Which is the true religion?” q 1 ],
nations of antiquity made remarkable progress
under false faiths. Surely no belief at all j s
better than an erroneous one ; so that, if th e
Greeks and Romans, while worshipping dozens
of now discredited divinities, transmitted to
posterity not alone model laws, but literary
and artistic masterpieces of unequalled excel-
lence, what might they not have done had they
but adopted the Jewish Jehovah as their God’
Yet, the decline of Rome might be almost said
to date from her acceptance of “ the true faith.”
Nor should it be forgotten that to this true
faith was owing the long millennium which
spread like a pall over Europe, substituting dirt
debauchery and ignorance for the enlightenment
that had gone before. With such a lesson
before us it is hard to admit the necessity for the
spread of true (dogmatic) religion in any country
young or old. Still, as we have conceded the
necessity, we have no desire to draw back. The
question now is to discover the genuine one and
expeditiously adopt it.
# # *
The quest, according to Mr, Jackson, is by
no means difficult. There can be but one true
religion. It is almost superfluous to add that
the Rev. H. Latimer Jackson has found it. R
is the parliamentanly-established Church of
England, whose first head was the matrimonially,
experienced Henry VIII., and whose coming
Rope is the Prince of Wales. With M. Tudor
and his harem at one end and M. Wettin and
his gambling-kit at the other, he would be a
bold person wdio questioned the sacred character
of the institution. At the same time it would
be idle to deny that such disagreeable and
sceptical people exist. The Nonconformists are
powerful in Australia; so are the Roman
Catholics; and so also are those who never enter
a church, except when brought to it by their
parents to be baptised, by some woman to he
married, or by their executors to be buried.
Each of these bodies derides the claims of the
English establishment, while the Salvation
Army flouts all. The Church of England,
according to the late Cardinal Newman, who
should.be an authority, having spent the best
years of his life in her service, is a schismatic
body whose members will suffer in the next
world for their contumacy in this. It may be
that in Australia the English Church is (on
paper) the strongest sect, but she is certainly
not so strong as all the others combined. Were
she subsidised, therefore, it would merely mean
the withholding of State aid from the majority
so as to confer it on a minority. It devolves on
Mr. Jackson then, to prove, not that his sect
embraces the majority of churchgoers, which is
impossible, but that it is the true one. He may
succeed in doing so, but in everyday language,
he has a hard row' to hoe.
# # *
Three courses are open : (a) discover the true
faith and subsidise it; (b) subsidise all; (c)
none. The last plan is that pursued in Greater
Britain—be., in English-speaking countries
beyond the United Kingdom, with the excep-
tion of India, which can scarcely as yet be con-
sidered a country of Anglo-Saxon speech. In
it there are English prelates and priests whose
success in everything beyond drawing liberal
salaries is still problematical. They no doubt
do their best to prove the superiority of Christ’s
teaching to that of Buddha or Brahma, but
the inhabitants of the great Asiatic dependency
are of a Conservative nature and do not take
readily to the truth either in religion or politics.
11l Ireland, too, the partnership between Church
and State—a partnership upheld by bayonets
tor centuries—has been dissolved ; while Wales,
Scotland, and even England itself show' unmis-
takable symptoms of a desire to terminate the
connection. The fact, then, that Church and
State do not go well together in harness is not
only acknowledged as an axiom among people
of British descent beyond the seas, but is
forcing itself upon the attention of millions in
Great Britain. Bearing this in mind, it is not
too much to concede to Mr. Jackson a certain
amount of courage in advocating the re-mar-
riage in Australia of this divorced and ill-
assorted couple.
* * *
Regarding the second course—that of subsi-
dising all churches, as the Catholic, Pro-
testant, and Jewish faiths are in France.
Assuming that all the people of Australia
were church-goers of some kind, the prin-
ciple is tantamount to taking money out of
one pocket and putting it in another. The
only scheme even partially consonant with
common sense would be one that divided the
entire subsidy among the ecclesiastical estab-
lishments in proportion to the attendance.
Upon the injustice of this method to the bulk
of the population who never voluntarily attend
a place of worship at all, except through curio-
sity, it is scarcely necessary to dwell. They,
the non-churchgoers, would naturally say, “If
you want priests pay tor them. We decline to
be taxed in order that you may have your
religion cheap—in order that you may rejoice
in choirs, surplices, and sermons w'hich we con-
sider relics of an ignorant past.” It would be
hard to find an answer to such an objection.
If a person finds that incense and platitudes are
essential to his present and future Avelfare it is
surely not too much to ask him to pay for them.
Indeed, his hesitation to do so, or his desire to
get non-believers to share the expense so that
he may obtain salvation at the lowest monetary
figure, is not without a suspicion of meanness.
Of a certainty, a sincere man who conscien-
tiously accepted the doctrine of sempiterna
happiness or misery, would not hesitate to
avoid the latter and make certain of the former
by drawing a cheque to assist in squaring m®
back-slidings. A story told by X ado mm
[O’Meara’s “Voice froni St. Helena ] 1
apropos. The Emperor speaks :
1 recollect when I was in Italy, a priest preaemngabou
a poor sinner who had departed this life. n
appeared before Gon, and he was required to if>' '
account of all his actions. The evil and the £00”
afterwards thrown into opposite scales to see whicn I
dominated. That containing the good proved nmen
lighter, and instantly flew up to the beam. I* 1 " UA

” His saint instantly took the hint, gathered
, e ther all the lime and stone, threw them into the scale
nf"s-ood which immediately preponderated ; the scale of
vil sprung up to the beam, and the sinner’s soul into
paradise at the same moment.
The priest’s story, we read, was given to the
congregation to induce them to help in building
a new convent. It had the desired effect as,
for some days subsequently, the neighbouring
roads were blocked with cart-loads of eleemosy-
nary stone and lime. To us it appears that if
the condemned sinner alluded to had induced
the Government to provide the lime and stone
instead of bestowing them himself, he would
still be in Hades, with a certainty of a long
stay. This may suggest to Mr. Jackson how
necessary it is that persons who believe in
Hades should themselves pay to keep out of it,
instead of begging their neighbours to pay for
them- #
gut the real crux of the question is to dis-
cover the true Church. Nothing is too good
for it when once discovered. During the 18th
century an English Catholic or a French Protes-
tant was sure of an unpleasant here and here-
aftcr —so far as the Church, backed rip by the
State, had a say in the matter. It is intrinsi-
cally stony-hearted to make a man pay for your
Church, and at the same time assure him that
he will be damned for adhering to his own.
Surely it is cruelty personified to punish him in
both worlds—in the present by seizing his
goods, in the future, by seizing his soul. Before,
therefore, a step forward can be taken in the
matter of establishing a State Church, it is
incumbent on Mr. Jackson to prove which is
the infallible one. This he, no doubt, can
readily do to his own satisfaction, but that is
not enough. He must do it to the satisfac-
tion of the Government and of the popular
majority. From what is known of the
present New South Wales Administration
and its leader, there can be little looked
for in that direction —even if the Labour-party
gave a block vote in favour of the scheme.
The chance of Sir Henry Pabkes, Premier of
Mr. Jackson’s province, establishing a parti-
cular sect for the purpose of preserving the
public morals, is improbable. Nor is there much
hope from the Opposition. The members of the
latter are too busily engaged in the effort to
establish themselves on the Speaker’s right, to
spare time for the adoption of any new beliefs.
So it would appear that even when Mr. Jackson
has succeeded in selecting the true faith, his
work will be but begun. The Fates are against
him from the start.
* * *
“ A country where God was not recognised
at all could never prosper nor reach true
national greatness.” So says Mr. Jackson,
Yet the French got on very well after the
enthronement of a cocotte as the Goddess of
Reason. They even pulled down thrones under
this droll tutelary—thrones, moreover, which,
according to the proclamations of their occu-
pants, had been founded and upheld An nom de
latres sainte et indivisible Trinite. In fact,it was
only after the Concordat with Rome that the
Gallic star began to wane. Besides, as pre-
viously hinted, the great nations of antiquity
prospered remarkably well under false gods,
although it is obviously better to have no
Deity at all than spurious ones. The Eastern
barbarians, with not a respectable divinity in
their Pantheon, sacked Rome, which possessed
not alone a richly-endowed State Church, but
relics of saints innumerable. So it would have
been with London in 1805 had the indolent
Villeneuve given Bonaparte what he wanted
—the command of the Channel for a few days.
Once landed in Kent or Essex, the whole
English army, headed by the Regent and full
bench of bishops, would have been powerless to
prevent a victorious march on Babylon. It is
to be feared that the soldiers who, a few
months later, smashed the European coalition
at Austerlitz would have made short work of
King, Lords and Hierarchy.
# * *
Mr. Jackson believes that “ the propagation
of religious truth is one of the ends of Govern-
ment,” that an Established Church was the
‘ ‘ practical recognition of the spiritual character
of the State,” and that “a perfectly free
church had not proved an universal blessing. ’
The “spiritual character of the State’
especially with the four Georges, not to men-
tion “Silly Billy ” and the prospective Edward
VII. as successive Popes—is very fine, ihc
Romanists, it is true, could boast the Borgias
and other beauties, but, leaving out the poison-
ing proclivities, we would back the Guelphs
against all-comers for spiritual guides. In
Great Britain alone it takes over seven millions
sterling a year to support the Establishment.
What does the nation receive in return ? I he
blocking of all liberal measures by a posse of
bishops who, if Christ appeared among them
to-day, would commit Him to prison as a vagrant
and an impostor. Thoughtful Englishmen are
gradually coming round to the opinion that the
worst thing which ever happened their country
was the non-appearance of Bonaparte’s bear-
skins in St. James’s and St. Giles’s. Unpat-
riotic to say so ? Bah ! Go to England and
see the rich rectors whose wives would not
touch a labourer with their fans —to Wales and
observe the greasy parsons with their _ bailiffs
and soldiers garnering the poor man’s crop
which they never sowed. They pounce upon
his barn in the name of God, and millions begin
to ask wdiy in God’s name the iniquity is
tolerated.
* * *
Free churches are not an unmixed blessing —
for the clergy. The latter have to work for
their living and are promptly told when they
are required no longer. What wonder that
Australian ecclesiastics should sigh tor the flesh-
pots of an Establishment ? The Apostles,
when preaching was slack, worked at their
trades to fill in time. What was good
enough for them and even for J esus
Himself is surely good enough for a
modern pastor. Suppose the State decided to
acknowledge an Establishment, but without
endowments of any kind. Would there be a
rush for appointments ! We smile. '1 here is
no opportunity at present to expose the fallacy
°f supposing that morality and what is called
religion go hand-in-hand. Over and over again
it has been shown that there is .no necessary
connection between the two, while there is a
very strong bond between morality and educa-
tion. Some of the world’s most exalted charac-
ters have professed either no faith at all or a
very indifferent one, while most of the world’s
moral monsters have been devotees. Mr.
Jackson has come to the wrong country at
the wrong time to impose on a free community
the tags and tatters of obsolescent superstition.
The “Mildura” Stokers.
The S.M. Herald, the champion explainer and
boss apologist of New South Wales, has risen
to wipe out and utterly abolish the latest dis-
graceful scandal connected with the alleged
“Australian” fleet. It has done it in the
manner for which the S.M. Herald is famous,
by first liquoring in an affable manner with the
Admiral, who stated that there was nothing at
all the matter with the famous Mildura, and
next liquoring cheerfully with the captain, who
repeated his assertion. Then the newspaper
representative appealed to the men, and as they
hadn’t liquored with anybody their complaints
were long and vigorous. And finally the press-
man seems to have been affably entertained by
somebody else, and going away with a com-
fortable sensation in his stomach he wrote two
columns setting forth that all the original
statements seemed to be true, but even if they
were true there was no reason why anybody
should have made a fuss. The temperature of
the stoke-hole was apparently the temperature
of a nether hell; the water didr run short; the
ventilating fans did break down ; sentries were
placed over the exits to prevent the perishing
slop-made wretches who were shovelling coal
below from making their escape into a purer
atmosphere ; and the whole blazing infamy of
the story was apparently true in every parti-
cular but—
If anybody is to blame it is that impersonal and un-
reachable body known as the Admiralty, and as we cannot
touch the Lords, we can do but little good by harping on
the subject.
Which same remark, made in a cheerful strain
by a well-filled man who isn’t thirsty, nor in
want of pure air, nor otherwise in trouble,
would fit equally well the stoke-hole of the
Mildura or the furnace of Gehenna, where
there is also “ little good ” in the lost and fried
soul “ harping on the subject.”
# # #
The following is a slab from the statements of
the men, which are not worth harping upon
The first spokesman, a leading stoker, complained of
the treatment he had received from the doctor two days
after leaving Suez. He had fallen ill, and had consulted
the doctor, who had refused to put him on the sick-list.
He was thoroughly fagged out with the work and heat,
and as an illustration of the effect of the voyage he stated
that he had lost 501 b. in weight since he left England. He
also complained of the sentries being placed on the air-
locks during the hottest part of the passage, and declared
that the stokehole bore a striking resemblance to an
“inferno.” The after stokehole was sometimes red-hot,
and scalding water washed about on the bilge plates.
Men had fainted and had been brought up on deck and
revived with sea-water. He had also been abused by the
chief engineer, and altogether, though he had been five
years at sea, had never known such a time before.
Another leading stoker complained of the abuse which
had been showered upon him. Though he had been
through the Red Sea before, he had never experienced
such heat as in the stokehole of the Mildura. A third
leading stoker stated—“ We were abused like dogs for
not keeping enough steam up.” When the fans broke
down it became exceptionally hot. These complaints
were repeated by most of the men, though sometimes,
during the lengthened inquiry, novel points of a minor
character were brought out. On one occasion the middle
watch at night was kept on duty until half-past 6 in the
morning, instead of until 4 only. This meant two and
a-half hours’ extra work.
That is all! There isn’t anything more ! The
stoke-hole was only red-hot, and the foul water
that washed around was a scalding fluid, and
the general sensation was that of sitting in a
boiling-kettle on top of a stove. The ventila-
ting apparatus had gone to pieces ; there was
an armed individual at the air-holes to prod
back anyone who wanted a breath even of the
burning air from the Red Sea, and a look at
the light of the brassy sky. And it was, in the
Herald s estimation, only a novel point of a
minor character, that the men who had been
shovelling coal for four hours in this demoniac
atmosphere should be compelled, as alleged,
through the blundering of some affable fool in
brass and buttons, to shovel coal for only two
and a-half hours longer. If the S.M. Herald’s
representative had been swinging a huge pair
of Indian clubs in the hottest room of a Turkish
bath for four hours without intermission, and
just when he wanted to crawl out and die,
somebody ordered him, in a perfectly friendly
manner, to stay in and swing the same clubs
for two and a-half hours more, he would pro-
bably understand the situation. That also
wouldn’t be worth harping on—that is to say,
it wouldn’t be worth while for anybody to harp
except the wretch with the fried back and the
baked head, who had been swinging the clubs.
This one item of the story has since been con-
tradicted by Captain Karslake, so the chances
for and against it are about equal, but when
the Herald account was published it was still
an undisputed assertion, and that papers
humane idea about “ points of a minor charac-
ter ” is worth noting.
* * *
Things so far were bad, but a demon of
explanation entered into the captain and the
admiral and made things worse. The admiral
speaks: —
Do you know that on board this vessel, the Orlando,
the average temperature of the stokehole is about 30 -
higher Ilia'll that registered on the Mildura? Only the
other day, on the passage from New Zealand to Sydney,
the thermometer in the stokehole rose to ICO - , and this in
a comparatively cool climate and duiing the wintti
season.
Here the question arises : If the temperature
rises to 160 degrees on board a vessel travelling
in cool latitudes in winter, who can reasonably
believe that it was only 134 in another vessel
travelling at high speed through the hottest
sea on earth ? Had the thermometer broken
down as w ell as the ventilating-fans ? Also,
is even 134 degrees a temperature at which any
individual one shade above a brute can require
any other man to do 6A consecutive hours of
exhausting labour? Further, if the condition
of the Mildura is bad, does it alleviate things
in any way to learn that the Orlando is worse ?
Would Admiral Scott feel inclined to sing
snatches of opera in a hot frying-pan simply
because Gehenna was still hotter i
Further explanations follow :
The ship is supposed to be capable of doing IS knot?,
but the most we ever ran teas 13, a little over hay M prco.
Of course I know our passage was more hurried than mat,
of the other vessels of the squadron. We were a tort-
night late in leaving England through causes over which
1 had no control, and by the orders of the Admiralty this
time had to be made up, in order that all the ships might
arrive in Australia together. I was thus compelled to
keep up speed in going through the Red Sea, whether I
liked it or no, but at no time was the vessel driven at
anything like full speed.
The question whether the captain did or did
not like fast steaming is interesting from an
outside standpoint, but that officer’s extraneous
views had no influence whatever on the tem-
perature in the stokehole. And in any case
a man who is kept working for from four to
six-and-a-half consecutive hours in a murderous
atmosphere cares no more for the captain s
opinions on fast steaming than he does about
the captain’s opinions on infant baptism. He is
in the stoke-hole all the same, and the hidden
sentiments of a man who is enjoying the pure
atmosphere above are of no account. Further,
if the Mildura becomes a floating perdition
when she is toiling along at a poor 13 knots an
hour, what is she likely to be when doing the
whole 18?—and she has been sent here on the
pretence that she can do 18 ? More explanation
ensues on other points :
The story about the sentries with loaded muskets and
fixed bayonets turns out to be rather highly coloured.
“ It is true,” exclaimed the captain, “ that on three or
four occasions a man was told off to watch the exit from
the stoke-hole, and for a very good reason. Constant
complaints were made about the habit which the men had
of bolting up from below, and thus shirking their work
altogether. . . . These sentries were simply men
belonging to the watch on deck, and were not armed at
all. How they came to be equipped with loaded rifles
and fixed bayonets rather puzzles me, and I can assure
you that such a thing was never even dreamt of.” As to
the scarcity of water after leaving Aden, the captain ex-
plains that the consumption had become so extravagant
that it was necessary to place some check on it. The
ship’s corporal was ordered to see that there was no un-
necessary waste; but at the same time everyone was
allowed to have as much as was realty needful.
In other words, the men complain that sentries
were placed to keep them below, and the
captain suavely admits the truth of the
story. The men also say that their water-
supply was short, and the captain blandly
acknowledges that he put them upon an allow-
ance to check “extravagant consumption,” as
that term is understood by a person who has
the run of all the cool liquors in the cabin.
They say the same thing in different ways.
The captain says it in a gay and affable way
because he was on deck, and the men say it
bitterly because they were below. The officer
bears up manfully under the stokers’ affliction,
and the stokers would probably have borne up
as manfully under his affliction if the positions
had been reversed. Also, if the reporter had
been below, and the stokers had been writing
for the S.M. Herald they might also have
thought the matter “ not worth harping upon.”
# * *
A few more details are thrown in here. The
blue-jackets—as the Herald man is careful to
mention—look well, and seeing they were in no
way concerned he might as reasonably have
added, for the purpose of refuting the accusa-
tionsmade, that the Patagonians seemed healthy,
and the Japanese had no complaints to make.
The captain of the Mildura seemed nervous
—why he was nervous isn’t stated. The
Admiral has ordered an enquiry, though why
he did that, seeing that he blandly admits
everything, Heaven only knows. He or his
subordinate also wrote to Sir Samuel Griffith
“ entirely denying the charges,” which seems a
somewhat steep thing to do, considering that
the charges are true by their own showing.
The whole exhibition was an amazing comedy.
The Admiral was in his cabin on the flag-
ship denying everything. The captain was
on deck, looking “nervous.” The S.M.
Herald reporter stalked around reviewing the
men, and taking evidence against the officers.
The stokers were on deck cursing the officers.
The blue jackets tacitly gave the captain a
testimonial as a well-meaning individual who
was hardly ever sick at sea. Then the news-
paper man blandly told the commander that he
was discharged without a stain on his character.
The proceedings ought to have closed by the
crew presenting the Admiral with a testimonial
in his cabin, and having a glass of wine with
him, and stating vocally that he was a jolly
good fellow, but somehow this ceremony was
omitted. Taking it all round it is a joyous
specimen of naval discipline when the fleet
rises up to explain itself to what the naval
Lords would no doubt contemptuously speak of
privately as a twopenny newspaper.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Broking Swindle.
The Brokers Bill, about to be introduced in the
Victorian Assembly, will, if passed, put
£20,000 worth of taxation on to the right
shoulders, and promote a healthy reform among
the gambling-sharks of the Stock Exchange. It
provides, in the first place, that all brokers
shall be licensed, and this arrangement will
exterminate in one act half of the swindlers and
jobbers who haunt the precincts of the ex-
changes. And, secondly, it provides that
every broker must keep an accurate record of
his transactions, and his books must be at all
times open to inspection. And when the
broker is always open to inspection there will
practically be an end of “spec, selling,” and
the borrowing of scrip, and the bogus sales in
which one man, who doesn’t possess a single
share, pretends to trade off 7000 of them in one
line to his confederate at a big advance, and the
confederate passes them on to his accomplice at
another advance, and so an artificial boom
is created when there is nothing to
justify it save a barren mountain-peak—
facetiously called a “mine”—and a lot of
cast-iron lying. For, in the matter of naked
and unadorned fraud, the ordinary broker
passes all comprehension. Often on the Ballarat
“ Corner,” or in the “ Beehive,” at Bendigo,
when nothing is doing and all is utter stagna-
tion, 30 or 40 of these stony perjurers will get
together in a heaving tumultuous mass, and
madly and furiously sell shares to each other by
thousands while the prices go up into a state of
frenzy. The mine dealt with is a dead-beat
hole; the sellers are dead-broke; the buyers
are stumped ; but for hours the crowd howl
and shriek, and tear up paper, and cast it over
each other, and rush into pubs, to drink, and
rush out again and buy more shares —20,000
shares at one shilling—the same shares at
2s. 6d.—the same shares over again at five
shillings —any number of shares at any price
under the sun. Night comes and finds them
still howling and buying the identical shares at
18s. 6d., and by this time the crowd is limp and
covered with perspiration, and its collar is
burst, and its shirt is a damp rag, and its boots
are trodden down at the heels with much leap-
ing and prancing. But in between whiles, some
flat wanders into the circle, and the crowd sells
shares to him in reality, with three days for
delivery, and then when it has caught all the
fiats that seem available it goes softly home to
tea. And next day it is wandering around, and
selling the same old lot of imaginary shares to
itself in a depressed sort of tone at sixpence,
and inside the three days it fills its orders at
waste-paper prices, and absorbs the difference.
Compared to the stock exchange swindle, even
the fan-tan fraud and the totalisator trick are
comparatively honest and harmless. Therefore
the Brokers Bill will doubtless commend itself
to many, and particularly to the godly gentle-
men who conduct operations on Melbourne
’Change, and have the front to inveigh against
gambling in between their other duties.

The Testimonial Complaint.
The testimonial-disease has spread even into
the British navy. The other day some news-
paper or other published a number of unkind
statements about the captain and officers
of the alleged man-of-war Tauranga, now upon
the Australian station, and thereupon the
crew rose up as one man, and presented the cap-
tain with a eulogistic document, since communi-
cated —no doubt, with the admiral’s sanction
to the daily press. It was a long and eloquent
publication, and woundup by stating that “ the
relations between the officers and men of Ibis
ship are of the most cordial description.” This
is the beginning of the new order of things.
Ten or twenty years hence every captain will
have a testimonial from his crew written in some
such strain as this
We, the undersigned seamen of H.M.S. Pannikin, hereby
testify that we have found Captain Smith a clean, moral,
and industrious officer, who has done his best to give
satisfaction. During the time he was in our employment
as commander we found him capable, cheerful, and
energetic, and also neat and tidy in his dress, and suave
in general conversation. He is a respectful and pains-
taking person, and under efficient supervision will always
he found useful. He is also always sober until 11 a.m.,
when the crew go ashore for the day, and invariably smokes
on the lee side of the ship, and his religious principles are
unexceptionable. We find it necessary to part witb him,
having made other arrangements, but can confidently
recommend him to any crew that may require his
services.
This is the sort of thing the testimonial business
always ends in. It starts on the assumption
that it is so surprising a British officer should
do his duty, that when he does it lie needs a
howling eulogium from everybody concerned ; it
spreads till the officer who doesn’t get a testi-
monial will presently be a blighted being ; and
it finishes in the apotheosis of drivel. It is like
goats, and drink, and influenza, and Chinamen,
for when once it begins it keeps on growing,
and unless the whole world swears off the thing
must wind up badly.
The servant-girl “cap” question involves the
whole subject of livery. Livery is slavery. A
compulsory uniforn is only necessary in the
cases of certain officers of the peace and
Government and railway officials who, when on
duty, become statutory officers of the peace.
The uniform is necessary then for public con-
venience and enlightenment. A policeman who
lias authority to stop and question a civilian is.
very propexdy required to wear a distinguishing
uniform or to be able to exhibit an official
badge. The same comment applies to customs-
officers, railway-guards, ships’ officers, &c.. &c.
But the livery of a private household is a demo-
cratic abomination and a badge of servitude out
of harmony with a free community. The
gaiters and cockade of a coachman, or the
gorgeous blue and yellow plush of a footman,
make manhood wail for its base counterfeits.
To submit to be compelled to wear the hideous
duds chosen by some rich and vulgar-souled
employer, in order to advertise that employer’s
supposed wealth and alleged social supremacy
over his flunkeys, is to admit that the wearer is
of inferior clay to the man who has purchased
his right to choose his own garments.

There are many strange anomalies in the legal
ring of New South Wales. Nobody is allowed
to practise unless he first passes the matricula-
tion exam, at the University, and then serves
five years under articles to a practising solicitor’.
In all the better class offices the solicitor charges
on an average, say, 200 guineas premium to
each articled clerk ; he pays them no salary or
one that is merely microscopic; and he is
allowed to have tlii’ee articled clei’ks at a time.
Consequently, he makes a steady income of
about 120 guineas per annum ; he gets his work
done gratis ; and the system, if worked for all
it is worth, admits of the whole gang of solici-
tors trebling in numbers evei’y five years.
English and Irish solicitors, and Writers to the
Signet from Scotland, are admitted without
examination, regardless of the fact tha't they
know no more of New South Wales law
than of the code of Timhuctoo. Scotch solici-
tors, who pass exactly the same examination in
Scotland as do the Writers to the Signet, are
not admitted ; they have simply to start life
afresh by going throxxglx the matriculation
exam, and being articled over again. The
cause of this amazing foolishness lias never been
explained, hut the Scotch solicitor pays a small
fee in his own country, and the Scotclx writer a
very large one, and probably the New South
Wales legal profession grovels by instinct to
the man who paid the big fee. Mr. Chick s
Bill, providing that anyone may become a
lawyer so long as he proves on examination
that lie knows law, is an astounding innovation l ,
for many reasons—among others, heeaxise,
under the present system, the solicitor is not
required to prove that lie knows law to any
extent. He lias to know all sorts of other
useless information, hut with the code itself he
has very often only a slight acquaintance. It
is an item that has been forgotten, somehow.

Mv Pear Moorabinua,—
Another large and logged-out army of smartyrs (copy-
right) appeared at Randwick on Oaks Day, but isn’t it
rather a reflection on “ sassiety ” that during the Spring'
Meeting the high places have been much more patronised
than the pit and gallery? Xo wonder the reformers can’t
get a “representative” bell-topper to extinguish the
totalisator under! The Govingment ’Ouse party was
there in all its glory, and I notice that their Excellencies
don't let everybody cavort round the vice-regal enclosure;
instead, there is a formal invitation to enter, when the
invited is never expected to squat on the cane furniture.
Lady Jersey (like hubby, she looks best dressed to death)
wore sap-green silk with a watered pattern in black, and
studded over with small pink flowers. The skirt was
demi-trained at the back over a foundation of spring-
green silk, the front had a deep black lace flounce high at
the sides and narrowing to the middle, caught with bows
of bright green ribbon ; the bodice had a pink silk vest
draped with black lace, and a small black lace and jet
bonnet had a coronet of unmounted blush roses. Lady
Kintore (who must run Bernhardt pretty close in the
matter of luggage) wore a black silk gown covered with
fine black Chantilly lace, trimmed with borders of shot
mauve, silk edged with steel beads ; small black jet and
lace bonnet with a purple flower set in aggressively at the
back, and black lace parasol. Lady Scott in a cornflower-
blue faille over a petticoat of faille edged with gold
braid, the bodice with a vest of 6cru faille veiled in
chiffon, small bonnet with crown of white jasmine. Mrs.
Rupert Carington wore a salmon-pink front and chiffon
frills on her black gown, toque en suite, and Manola cloak
in black and gold braided yoke covering her glory
occasionally. Lady Innes wore a ruby and white-chintz-
patterned gown made en panier and trimmed with ruby
ribbon, velvet and white guipure, small bonnet trimmed
with red currants. Mayoress Manning wore a cool-look-
ing cream delaine figured in heartsease and trimmed with
bows of grey and mauve ribbon, black bonnet lightened
by a fall of gold-dusted black tulle ; Miss Manning had a
bunch of daffodils in her white bodice, and a wide hat
trimmed with wattle and black velvet. Carrie Swain,
in black and steel beads, was the counterpart of Nellie
1 arren in the first scene of “ Ruy Bias ; ” and perhaps the
most correct get-up on the members’ stand was a pale-
grey match even to the Bond-street hat trimmed with
velvet and the little gauntlet gloves—the only colour being
a flesh-pink carnation in the buttonhole and a flesh-pink
parasol.
Craven Plate day at Randwick was an off-day in every
sense but a barometrical one, for the weather was gor-
geous if the worldlings weren’t. There wasn’t a vice-regal
woman anywhere ; for one cause and another the Aus-
tralian Club ball had been kept up the night before till
cockcrow.
Nellie Barren was the attraction on the members’
stand, wearing a black broche frock trimmed with beads,
under a Manola wrap in black velvet with yoke and collar
and edge of gold lines ; toque of black velvet and jet with
touch of red in it. Nellie, by the way, is gotten-up better
than she used to be for out-door use, and walks the lawn
with the same little fussy toddle she wears on the stage.
Another gasping-stock was the wealthy little Melbourne
brunette who shone out at the Australian Club hop,
dontcherknow, and who is worth I don’t know how many
O’a after the first figure of her dot. She wore a yellow
muslin startlingly adorned with cornflowers, and a black
crinoline hat trimmed with those simple flowers of the
field en suite. Miss Dight wore cream muslin with a
yellow spot, trimmed with airy yellow chiffon ; dead white
hat with yellow flowers. Miss Cox was in cream muslin
with satin ribbons and cream and yellow hat. Miss Fos-
bery’s neat chow costume in lilac gingham looked good
style, but a little dollish woman was fancy-fair-ish in a
grey satin trimmed with silver-spangled tulle ; a maiden
in a white jacket with a nosegay in her collar suggested
that she was Queen of the May, mother ; but the queerest
figure there had its hair short, spectacles and sailor hat
a rigid frock in fawn tweed made with a dicky front, race
glasses strapped across, and a man’s umbrella ! But the
funniest thing about it was that it behaved so decorously
that it might have been its own maiden aunt all the time.
This Spring A.J.C. Meeting was a very hilarious out-
break, and it died game with the biggest attendance of
all on the last (Steeplechase) day. There wasn’t any
vice-regal pivot (one doesn’t count the “ suite ”)and it’s
astonishinghow smoothly things went off even with desola-
tion in the vice-regal box. The music was very good and
lured people to promenade and show off their wearing
apparel. Miss Buckley (Melbourne) had on a very com-
plete turn-out in mauve silk with a circle design in white
trimmed with guipure embroidery, white chiffon ruffles,
toque of green velvet bows, and wreath of Russian violets;
her parasol of the same stuff as her frock. Miss Gowland
embodied the aphorism of “beauty unadorned ” in white
muslin sprigged over with little grey flowers and tied
round with a simple white sash ; a handsome frock was in
sage-green, the cuirass drapery of closely woven flesh-pink
lace studded with “ rubies; ” a heliotrope with the top part
made like a frock-coat, the collars lined with white silk,
was an ugly novelty. Miss Hack (South Australia) had
a pretty frock of pinkish muslin with a deep lace basque,
her leaf-straw hat trimmed with pink and touches of sage
green. This fair damsel, who sings like a syren, is
affianced—did 1 tell you?—to the tall Dr. Alaster Cox.
Mrs. (Colonel) Mackenzie in cobweb grey, in a grey bonnet
sunning o’er with buttercups ; Miss Ada Fosbery in pink
had a three-cornered hat in black, trimmed with little
white flowers, her “ chum’’—Miss Nellie Hill—in straw-
berry tweed with rough white lines trimmed with gold ;
smart little bonnet in green velvet, gold tinsel and what’s-
o’-clocks. There were several coaches on the last d»v_
Mr. Terry's spick and span bays taking the polish off the
other four-in-hands, even to the women on the roof.
The vice-regal garden-party on ICthinst. was a crowded
and successful affair, even though the weather was rather
too uncertain for a. fete champetre. Yet everybody turned
out in “ ribbons gay ” and was received by Lord and Lady
Jersey, and the bonny (of course the printer will make it
“the bony”) Kintore and brother Rupert No. 1, who all
stood on the steps of the piazza and welcomed the com-
pany in a sort of how-d'ye-do-de-quatre - a port-wine
magnolia tree close by shedding a sweet perfume over the
ceremonies. The “ goodies,” for which the usual hungry
rush was made, were set out in the covered-in verandah,
and a band jerked Sullivanisms most of the time while
the guests (who had been meeting one another and making
talk day and night for the past week) tried to look as if
they had something fresh to say and were delighted with
the chance. As for the garments, lots of soothers came
in at the garden-gate. Lady J. was rather haphazard in
a lilac frock with darker lines and a coral pattern over it
in white, cuffs and front of Maltese lace and mauve
velvet, mauve gloves and a rather scrunehed-looking
bouquet, which, of course, didn’t harmonise-—presenta-
tion bouquets never do. Lady Barley sat on the piazza
all the time, keeping her black and gold bonnet out of
the wet, presumably ; Lady Innes wore a charming dress
of green Bengaline embroidered at the border and trimmed
with black ribbon velvet and gold gimp, black ostrich boa
and small black jet bonnet. The Mayoress wore snow-
flake tweed combined w ith electric-blue ; Mrs. (Dr.)Twy-
nam was in electric-blue silk covered with black-figured
grenadine; Miss Dight was artistic in pink tweed with a
brown silk vest and red currants in her hat; Miss Gow-
land, in china-blue dotted woollen trimmed with blue
Bengaline and gold gimp, looked really nice. Talking
w’as the amusement, and one heard the low murmur of
culchaw on every hand. For instance, one gentleman
socially remarked to a lady as the crowd surged some-
what: “ I hope you left your corns at home, Mrs. Dash.”
Talking about the garden • party, it was very
amusing to see a popular vice-regal visiting secretary
sneaking cautiously into Government House by the back
way in very Pickwickian clothing (faded tweed with a
little bit of braid off, the sort of thing one’s big brother
goes a-flshing in), but emerging from the front door a
few minutes later in all the glories of pot-hat and red car-
nation ! How he must envy the lightning-change artist
who can do a little juggle like that in full view of the
audience!
Mayoress Manning’s “at home ” (dancing), held in the
vestibule of the Town Hall on 17th inst., was quite a suc-
cessful festivity attended by lots of pretty women in neat
frockings, which, with the garlands of evergreen, palm-
leaves and festoons of terra-cotta silk on the walls, made
up into a rather gay picture. The Mayoress (who enjoys
the polka) wore a bodice and train of Francisca-coloured
brocade with jupe of cream moire with flounces at waist
and hem of cream lace; sleevelets and folds of cream
chiffon held by a diamond clasp ; the prettiest turn-out
was of dead-white silk and lace, the front flounce studded
with little white flowers, a large posy of exquisite
daffodils the only colour. Miss M. Windeyer wore a
white silk gown trimmed with white ostrich-feathers.
Miss Marks’ white satin was trimmed with silver
gimp, lace and trails of honeysuckle. A very pretty
pompadour brocade (blue, with design in pink)
the corselet bodice finished with folds of pink and
blue crepe, was economically cut above the waist-line, and
rather spoilt as a turn-out by a “stage” diamond neck-
lace ; Mrs. T, Slattery’s gown was an artistic juggle of
yellow brocade, old gold velvet, old guipure and un-
dressed ostrich feathers, of which her fan was composed ;
a very pretty girl was dowdy in a pinky-yellow frock ;
Miss Halliday in salmon-pink silk, gold gimp and chiffon ;
and a doctor’s pretty wife in an art costume of cream
brocade, wore her skirts quite an inch too short frontally,
which rather spoilt her ensemble in the square dances,
you know. I did not remain until supper, which, I am
told, was a dazzling achievement in keeping with the floor
and music (piano and fiddlers three) which were quite
tip-top.
The very latest Potts Point way to treat your kiss-curls
is not to curl them at all, but to have them combed out
straight and scanty in a powdered line across your baby-
brow. This has probably come in because hot irons and
damp "pins” have made the hair come out, and I hear
the correct way to fix it is with a small tooth-comb—the
teeth of which instrument it very much resembleth, by
the way.
The second Spring ball at Sydney Government House
on ISth inst. consisted of the usual equal number of men
and women, flowers, floor, food and fiddling, and people
must sometimes long to be invited to walk on then-
hands to the tune of tom-toms with a bread-and-water
buffet just for a blessed change. With a half-concealed
caligraphic yawn (your Sappho has been stifled with a rag
fair for the last fortnight) 1 inform a listening hemisphere
that the titled hostess on this occasion appeared smiling
in yellow faille enriched with beaded relievements,
diamond suite ; the South Australian social guardian-
angel wearing mauve-blue brocade softened with chiffon ;
diamond jewels. Sixteen dances—including a schottische
—were elasticised by extras. And, talking of dancing,
the long, loping, languorous waltz has quite gone out
among the beacon-lights of sassiety, who now take jig-
steps to time. Also polkas, in which the dancers hop as
high as Hainan, have a big say at present on the “ toney’>
dance-card.
The two swell Sydney club balls took place last week,
the Australian leading on 16th inst. in the Centennial
Hall. Imagine hysterics over the greenery and the
gobbling, which were both an’artist’s inspiration—especi-
ally the little encampment of tents surrounded by
shrubs in the vestibule wherein the parted partners
found rendezvous all the evening. Lord and Lady
Kintore and Sir Precedent Scott were the dais deities on
this occasion. The Union Club held their annual hop on
18th inst., and great was the success thereof. Lord and
Lady Jersey were both on the floor and couldn’t tear
themselves away until 12.30, so nearly everybody saw
the programme through, determined to enjoy, to the
final bar, the last big festivity of the gay and festive race-
week.
A drawing-room wedding had Miss Mary MTlvvraith—
second daughter of Sir Thomas M'llwraith, of “ Auchen-
flower,” Queensland, to Mr, Montague Talbot Stanley,
eldest son of Mr. F. D. G. Stanley, Toowong, Queensland,
on 9th inst. The wedding was, as weddings should be, a
very quiet one, only old friends and relatives being pre-
sent, and the bride wore white brocade trimmed with
cord and chiffon, and a'.very choice posy. The two maids
(Misses MTlwraith and Stanley) in white book muslin and
Valenciennes lace. Sydney honeymoon, the bride setting
out upon it in grey crepe cloth embroidered in silver.
An historically interesting match was made at Sutton
Forest on 9th inst. (when Miss Kate Nicholson, daughter
of Mr. C. L. Nicholson, of “ Newbury,” Sutton Forest,
was married to Mr. Fred. N. Oxley, late of “Kirkham,”
Camden), since the grandfathers of both parties came to
Australia long ago as naval officers, the bridegroom’s
grandpa being a rather adventurous explorer and first
Surveyor-General of N.S.W. Mrs. Oxley will reside at
Oxford, where her husband is Resident Engineer.
Sydney Liedertafel shriek, on 15th instant, was a calm
and peaceful way of spending the evening of the Metrop.
Day—the Holy Supper of the Apostles” (by Wagner)
falling- like dew upon the levered tympanums of peop’e
deafened by the loud odds of the paddock. However,
some of the slippering: apostles sang as if bellowing a
rival down was their object, too, and whether it is that
we aren’t soulful enough, or the “apostles ” miss fire at
critical moments—when St. John reaches across Peter for
the butter as it were, or St. Matthew puts his knife in his
mouth, in a manner of speaking—remains to be fought
out by the critics. Miss Lalla Miranda, a Victorian
songstress, who has greatly improved since she debutted
with Patey, in Melbourne, last November—was loudly
encored for her numbers, but her selection of “ Auld
Robin Gray ” was unwise, since that pathetic illustration
of matrimonial expediency (nobody ever wastes any
emotion over Robin, I notice) is altogether unsuited for a
soprano voice.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.

Business with the Gaiety Co. suffered a
slight drop during the middle of the week, though
nothing to that experienced at other houses, but
the theatrical mercury has since risen to “ set
fair,” and seems likely to remain there. Meyer
Lutz’s arrangement of “The Bogie Man,”' 0 f
which we heard so much during last pantomime
season, does not seem an improvement on the
American setting, and Leslie’s method in it
does not compare favourably with that of little
Ayrton at the other Imperially-named house.
I lorence Levey fell down half-a-dozen stairs the
other night at the feet of her Danby, who had no
appropriate gag ready for the unexpected
declension. Subjects of general comment are the
realistic hue of Nellie Barren’s flesh-tights in the
crossing-sweep act, and the admiring glances that
a certain shapely, pink-limbed damsel, apparently
“shook on her own shape,”continuously throws
down on herself. Perhaps, however, it’s modesty—
she may be abashed by the steady glare of the bald-
heads’ row. Some of the gags, particularly those
built up on the names of the performers, get more
and more wire-drawn as the nights spin out, and
the small talk of the chorus up-stage, when the
alleged bull-tight is on outside the wings, is abtmt
the poorest attempt at description in this line ever
known. Folks do not seem particularly gone on
any of the Gaiety songs ; some are not new, ail
are vulgar. Anything more inane than “Just
had a wire to say so ” and “Wink the other eye ”
can scarcely be imagined. But, truth to tell, even
the most unrefined' Cockney alleged witticisms
seem to go down without greasing even in the
circle. Leslie freely describes certain dance steps
as “rotten,” and alludes to objects of dislike as
“rotters.” By the way. Leslie might get a lot
more fun out of his dual Scotch - Irishman.
“ Cinder-Ellen ” is promised on Monday, Oct. 5.
** * *
“ The Village Priest ” is one long
appeal to the mystic from draw-up to riug°-
down ; but religion always provides its consola-
tions, the good Abbe never sends round the col-
lection-plate. That’s where Grundy and nature
part company. According to report the writer
took the idea from Busnach and Canvin’s “ Le
Secret de la Teneuse,” but remodelled, did not
adapt, the drama. Anyhow, the play finds its
cause in the wicked work of Judge d’Arey who,
after the fashion of his successors, intrigued with
the Comtesse de Tremeillan, and, fearing dis-
covery, shot her husband and his friend, while in
his judicial capacity sentencing an innocent game-
keeper, Torquienie, to prison for the murder.
Then he goes home to die of apoplexy after con-
fessing his guilt to the Abbe, and the play begins
with the betrothal of the Judge’s son, Armand,
to the daughter of his father’s mistress. Then
the gamekeeper escapes and confides his story to
Armand, who, in his researches, discovers his
father’s guilt and the secret of his prospective
mother-in-law. The good Abbe, troubled for
years over the incarceration of the innocent,
finds fresh cause for mental trouble in the
now broken-off engagement of the young folks
and the repulse of the escapee by his daughter,
who has been cared for since childhood by the
Judge’s widow. The moonlight falling through
the cloister window on his open Bible and an
appropriate text decides him, and he reveals the
secret of the confessional. The innocent criminal,
however, fearing to wound the woman who had
succoured his child, refuses to be cleared, and goes
back to gaol with the guilt of her husband still
attached to his name. So the story is completed
in a style that upsets all the traditions of the
playwrights’art. Everything is incomplete. The
guilty woman continues to repent her sin and
bewail its consequences ; her daughter is still
kept apart from the now wretched son of
the wicked Judge, and the gamekeeper’s
daughter is left to bewail her innocent father’s
cruel fate. In fact, the only comfortable creature
is the Judge’s widow, full of belief in her late
husband’s goodness. The sombreness of this play
is not lit up by one touch of comedy. It contains
more woe and desolation to the act than even
Pinero’s wearisome effort to be serious in “The
Profligate.” Chas. Cartwright’s Abbe looks more
like an Irish than a French priest, but he suc-
ceeds admirably in holding his audience while he
ladles out long sermons, ably penned, but invari-
ably wearying and depressing. Olga Nethersole,
for the first time in her Sydney experience,
handles a middle-aged woman. Her make-up is,
however, decidedly youthful, and the powder
flying from her hair in tragic moments caused
folks on the stage to sneeze and those off to grin.
Her best chance occurs at the end of the third
act, and here, on first night, the little lady was
so overwrought that she became hysterical and
couldn’t respond to a thundering call from a
crowded house. Cautley’s escapee is well played,
but disfigured by the convulsive jerks, like those
of a wire-impelled marionette, with which he
draws his legs after him. Cosgrove is marring his
chances by picking up the peculiarities of those
with whom he is associated. All the other
people, especially Mary Kingsley, are acceptable.
“Moths” will be played for Miss Nethersole’s
benefit to-night (Thursday), and the Darrells, as
previously announced, open on Saturday in “ The
Sundowner.” Owing to the indisposition of Miss
Nethersole, the Cartwright combination will pass
the days that precede their Melbourne season at
the Mountains, instead of in a Brisbane theatre.
