The F. B. WHEELER CO.,
218 Castlereagh Street, Sydney.
“Led Astray” does not seem to have
revived the languid business lately prevailing at
Sydney Criterion. Yet the play is magnifi-
cently staged and costumed, and excellently
played. It is one of those Boucicaultian
dramas that remind one of Lever’s novels,
abounding, as they do in Irish squires and
captains, French nobles, and adventurers of both
sexes. This play deals, however, chiefly with the
dangers that environ a wife whose husband ima-
gines that the protection of his name and the
use of his banking-account ought sufficiently
to satisfy all requirements. The temptation
comes in the person of a lovely young poet,
George de Lesparre, ably played by Percy Lyndal,
who is roaming the earth in search of his affinity
and who finds it in the person of the Baroness
Ohandoce (Mrs. Brough), the second wife of a gay
sportsman (George Titheradge), the father of an
innocent and sweet young girl, nicely played by
Miss Spong. The lady, however, is not so much
“led astray ” as is the punctilious husband who,
believing that what is merely folly in the man is
absolute crime in the woman, finds her guilty of
some undefined offence, against the evidence of
his senses. The couple are at length re-
united through the efforts of the little daughter
and her fiancee, a good-natured busy-body,
cleverly played by Dot Boucicault. George
Titheradge’s is the performance; the comedy, of
which Dot Boucicault is the backbone, is received
with shrieks of laughter, and Mrs. Brough is quite
equal to the calls made on her capacity for pour-
traying emotion. Edward Sass, as the fire-eating
Irish swindler, is a bit uncertain in tongue,
and Louise Davenport makes her re-appearance
as his accomplice, after a long absence
from the Sydney stage. Other gaps are ably
filled by Annie Taylor, Constance Deorwyn and
Mr. Chichester, but one or two characters like
that of the faithful retainer might be advan-
tageously eliminated.
* * Hf *
“ A Celebrated Case ” was put up by that
celebrated actress, Laura Villiers, and her cele-
brated Co., at Sydney Her Majesty’s, on Saturday
night last, before an attenuated house. So far it
does not seem likely that this concatenation of
celebrity is likely to hit Sydney folks harder than
it did those in Melbourne. The play, run here by
Bandmann as “Proof,” we fancy, is not particu-
larly strong, and the manner in which Miss
Villiers, the murdered wife of the first act,
becomes her sorrowing daughter in the second,
proved somewhat bewildering to an easily-
muddled Saturday night public. The lady
“ star ” knows all the tricks of the trade
but seems to lack power and definiteness; her
death-scene in the prologue, artistically managed,
was her best effort. The leading man, Clifton
Anderson, with a fine voice and good presence, is
often inaudible and usually stagey, and the sup-
port generally is not of a character that would
entitle it to demand, or receive, first-class passage-
money, out and home, from any Australian
manager. Indeed, so far as we have been per-
mitted to judge, “Poverty Point” and Mac-
quarie-street South reek of more experienced and
capable performers. It is little wonder that the
old-time mummer growls at the importation of
“ talent.” On opening night the best performance
was that of Geo. P. Carey, an old identity, as the
typical, low-comedy stage-irishman, one of the
sort that Boucicault used to wedge in everywhere.
“ A Celebrated Case ” is said to be by “ Cazaran,”
but the whereabouts or status of that individual
and his motive for writing or adapting “ A Cele-
brated Case.” have not as yet come under our
notice.
“The Democrat,” a new and original
drama by Edward Duggan, produced at Sydney
Standard last Saturday night, fizzled out after a
disastrous season of one consecutiveevening, despite
the fact that Frank Cates, as “ a gambler-roue,”
was starred on the bills in conjunction with the
Eureka Stockade, the defence and capture of
which historic enclosure provided the central sen-
sation. On Monday night, prior to returning the
money, the author came before the curtain, and,
thanking those present (about 100) for their sup-
port, informed them that his play had been pro-
mised the support of the Trades and Labour
Council and Socialists’ League. Then embracing
the house with a comprehensive swoop of his arm,
he asked bitterly : “ Where are they?” To which
query a raucous voice from the wings replied
sorrowfully : “ Thirty-five is paid.”
** * *
Alf. Wyburd's Bondi Aquarium has risen
again from the ashes and sludge and combusted
fish which were the sole remains of the old-time
structure, and is to be re-opened on sth October.
Thirty-five new artists, including the famous Faust
Family, will prevade the festive scene; there will
be new water, new tanks, new fish, and a new
insurance policy, but there won’t be a new
Wyburd because Nature made only one, and has
never yet got her second wind after the effort.
The wild tinned salmon, the octopus, the shark,
and the pale and reserved salt herring will be on
the job, and there will be two orchestras and
refreshments, and all the other things which tend
to keep man up in this vale of tears.
** * *
Sydney Sunday shows continue to fill up
what would otherwise be to many estimable citi-
zens a howling blank in the day of rest. The
Alathea sails out with Arthur Gordon’s Sunday
concert parties twice a day; promenade concerts
are given in the Darlinghurst Rink under the
management of Alderman “Jimmy” Rainford;
Frank Smith gives afternoon performances at
Botany, Sunday night shows at the Alhambra,
and Standard Theatre concerts take place under
fhe patronage of the Trades and Labour Council.
** * *
At the Cyclorama, thousands attend
weekly to hang entranced with wobbling eyeballs
oyer the platform railings while the sugared sylla-
bles of Lecturer Beresford steal gently in at the
Portals of their least deaf ears.
** # *
The musical public of Melbourne continue
to support Italian opera largely and vociferously
to the extent of a shilling a time, whilst business
in the dress-circle and stalls is solid on Cuttica
nights. The Signora of that ilk is now generally
recognised as the trump-card of the pack, and,
when she and her husband are both doing their
level best to prove that the voice is mightier than
the big drum it is indeed well to be there.
“Lucia” was summoned to the rescue of the
treasury twice, instead of once, during last week,
and any further returns of the same complaint
would be borne with cheerful resignation. The
entire musical performance of that opera is so
good that the finger of scorn almost refuses to
waggle itself at Robert Kennedy’s absurd legs,
and the apathetic glare with which the chorus
regard events of great pith and moment can be
partially forgiven. Operatic choruses are mere
musical automata all the world over - . Flourish a
baton at the figures and notes will issue from their
slots. Otherwise they come, stand around, and
depart like lost sheep who have erred and strayed
from the waxworks. “ Rigoletto,” produced under
a lowering sky on Saturday night, was another
fine burst of harmony that elicited thunders of
applause, relieved by bitter reflections on the
scenery and accessories. Melossi sings and acts
like an artist in the name-part. There are no
Hies on this notable baritone performance, and
though the jester himself has the hump, he never
gives it to his audience. Signora Cuttica delighted
everyone with her Gilda; also the Dook (Signor
C.) contrived to warble, with a bad cold, consider-
ably sweeter than any ordinary nobleman whose
tubes are in fair working order. For the rest,
there was a grating sound about Cavalleri’s con-
tralto, which chips in for the famous quartet in
the last act, but Travaglini, as the murderous
carver of Gilda, and sundry minor vocalists, were
fully worthy of their shares in the occasion,
“Rigoletto” will certainly give a big lift to the
Alexandra fortunes, and should be good for seven
or eight nights in all. For Wednesday and
Thursday (to-night) the bill is “ Norma,” with the
other company at work. Guidotti, unfortunately,
is still on the sick-list, but is expected to be able
to replace her locum-tenens, Madame Boema, next
week.
** * *
Immortal Shakespeare was welcomed in a
worthy style at Melbourne Royal on Saturday.
The “wholesome fun” of three centuries ago is
not quite what it used to be, neither do the puns
of the Elizabethan era strike one nowadays as
they struck the Virgin Queen who probably heard
them for the first time, and thought them more
diverting than execution. Nevertheless there is
a cheery ring about “ The Merry Wives _of
Windsor,” and the spectacle of a fat man being
smothered in the soiled underclothing possesses
an enduring charm. That the old farcical
comedy, with its dainty, fantastical finale, will
boom here as it boomed erstwhile in Sydney is
more than possible. Coming after a desolated
season of melodrama, it seemed to bring sunshine
back to the well-beloved house on Saturday, and
the receptions given to the Rignolds and Miss
Kate Bishop were full of promise. The pic-
torial success of the entertainment was undeniable.
Preconceived notions of Falstaff, Ford, his fair
mistress, and a few other people are realised as
the characters come forward. If they interest on
account of their Shakespearian associations rather
than amuse with their mediaeval business and
harmless horseplay, the author is alone to blame.
And nobody dreams of blaming that defunct
Divinity for fear of being hanged, drawn and
quartered, by order of the societies that manu-
facture “papers” to his memory. William
Rignold’s fat knight naturally took chief acting
honours, with Kate Bishop and brother George a
couple of lengths in the rear, Frank Harcourt,
Stirling White, Jewett, Tolano, and Miss Watts-
Phillips put in good efforts whenever they found
an opening, but the representatives of Nym and
Pistol seemed to run a bit wild, and Simple—
handicapped with an idiotic part—cut up inglori-
ously. Dame Quickly, too, would shine better if
she forgot to rub her hands together just every
now and then. Some of the Sydney scenery is
small for the present stage, but the Windsor Park
setting fetched a full round of applause.
** * *
“Jane” goes on lying like the average
prospectus at Melbourne Bijou, where the nightly
attendance is good enough for the management to
rest contented with for another week at least.
“Jane” has no difficulty in mopping up the tear-
drop that starts from every “Hook and Eye”
performance. The transparent skin of Eille Nor-
wood’s comedietta is so thin as to expose the
mechanism inside, and one can see his dreadful
little jokes being worked up to a distressing
climax. Eille’s sense of humour is indicated by
the fact that he plays the hero of this one-act
atrocity, and figures as the victim of an innocent
deception which no sane virgin would dare to
practise upon a new-born babe, lest it might
avenge the insult by giving her a good hard knock
with the feeding-bottle. However, Eille, to do
him justice, evidently entertains some suspicion
that he is making an object of himself, and the
restraint he puts upon his abandon is exceedingly
strong. “Hook and Eye” has one redeeming
point, all the same. It is better than “ Twenty
Minutes Under an Umbrella.”
** * *
At the Princess’ a strong reaction has set
in, after about fourteen weeks of uninterrupted
prosperity. Weaker shows than “The Late
Lamented ” have fattened on a generous public
ere now, yet they seem to avoid this moral story
of bigamy and ’ jungle-cramps with unnecessary
unanimity. The tout ensemble has improved many
degrees since the opening night, especially in the
direction of Maggie Moore and Snazelle, and in
spite of its mosaic-work bizarreness the perform-
ance is enjoyable. Amongst several jokes not
fully appreciated is Snazelle’s attack of jungle-
cramps, intended as a travestie of the collywobbles
which disturbed poor H. M. Stanley’s interior on
the wedding morning, and put a mustard-plaster,
as it were, upon that hardened nigger-slayer’s
honeymoon felicity. “The Lamented” having
missed the financial mark, it is announced to be
withdrawn next Saturday in favour of “Kindred
Souls,” a genteel variety entertainment quite out
of place at the Princess’.
#* * *
The halls, &c., are ringing the changes on
their “fresh attractions” with solemn persistency,
and General Booth has begun a season to over-
flowing houses at the Exhibition, where people
can enter free and pay when they feel disposed.
Olympia, a new hall in Flinders-street, will be
formally opened next Saturday with an Inter-
national Beauty Show. A number of local Venuses
are being tinted up for the occasion, and if the
shilling-fee includes a squeeze all round, this
novelty ought to clutch on pretty considerable.

The Gaiety Company noted a falling-off in
the supply of social diversions during their second
visit to Melbourne. A leading member of the
talent remarks that they didn’t ’ave nearly so
many h'outings as before.
At Poverty Point.
An American paper spends about half a column of
space over Dora Wiley, “ the sweet singer of Maine,” and
tells a thrilling yarn about her success in Australia. The
present writer remembers that success. In was one
Monday night in Sydney Gaiety. The orchestra was on
strike ; so was the chorus, but at last the piano and the
French horn relented, and the four ugliest chorus-girls
were squared, and the opera commenced 45 minutes late.
The four aged chorus-girls tried to form two tableaux of
three each and failed, and they nearly danced their legs
off, while George Dean —if we remember rightly—as an
Italian monarch, tried to rally up the other performers
behind the scenes. Just as the chorus was about to faint,
Mr. Dean came on regretfully, and announced that the
show was burst. Miss Dora Wiley was doing six simul-
taneous hysterics and one faint in the dressing-room.
Manager Grant got up and fled when he saw the audience
coming to claim their money back ; he had used the
scanty takings to square the three aged chorus-girls, and
get the performance started, and \\ hen the mob bore down
on him he wisely sprinted round the corner. Then the
audience started to knock the end out of the theatre, but
Larry Foley came up and fought the whole crowd single-
handed in the vestibule, and cleared them out. That was
the only successful night the Dora Wiley opera company
had in Sydney, and that laclj—who was the worst failure
in the show—seems to have travelled on the “ ad.” ever
since.

Last week’s show business in Melbourne was about the
worst recorded for many a year. With the Opera House
closed, there were beggarly attendances at the Royal
(also shut up on Friday), yards of bare benches at the
Princess’ and Alexandra, and generally plenty of seats
vacant for “Jane,” at the Bijou.
That Patti can now earn £lOOO per night at concert
singing—four times the price she commanded when her
voice was in its prime—is almost incredible. Patti, after
all, was never so phenomenal as a vocalist pure and
simple, her value lay rather in the combination of three
exceptionally attractive qualifications —voice, appearance
and acting-ability—for playing operatic heroines. Twenty
years ago there was little or nothing to choose between
the ballad-singing of Albani, Patti and Nilsson, and it is
certain that the diva’s voice has dropped a note or two
since then. But Patti remained young whilst her former
rivals grew old, and left no one else to dispute her
supremacy and cut up the trade. The woman is a
gigantic monopoly. She has made a “ corner ”of herself,
and sells only small parcels of sweet sounds at famine
prices.
In the pantomimes this year the fairy prince will be
recognised by the baccarat counters in his hinder pocket.
Mrs. Brown-Potter was in Paris at latest, attending her
sister’s marriage with a Goldbug—like her own husband.
“There are only 40 good stories in the world, and 39 of
them are unfit to tell to ladies”—but, then, they go and
see the plays, and cui bono ?

“ Lucia di Laramermoor,” ascribed to the popularity of

Cause celebre coming in Melbourne by a “toff” against
a leading burlesque actor, for chucking out from behind
the scenes, with sundry damages to face-handle, integu-
ments, and honour, besides being made to look small in
the eyes of Nellie, Florry, and Sylvy.
Apropos of Hopetoun’s no beard. When Harwood
returned from England he addressed a big house at the
Royal, wearing his long black beard, which he chopped off
immediately, and appeared 15 minutes after, clean shaved,
as Cassandra, “Siege of Troy” burlesque.
The premiere, or first night, is usually the grand one
with a piece, but the Gaiety troupe introduces a system
of last nights, far outdoing the premiere. This begun in
London, with the send-offs to Australia, and the farewell
night, at Melbourne Princess’s was of the same kidney,
whether cunningly lashed up by the management,
deponent sayeth not. However, it was quite a glorified
music-hall show, with free conversation over the foot-
lights, and response to songs called for by the gallery.
Scene-painter Brunton has cut off his long and flowing
locks. He was tired of being pointed at as a Texas
cowboy.
It is related that ere the much-mashed Kate Castleton
had been in ’Frisco ten days she made a big sensation in
front of the theatre some time after the show. A gentle-
man was about to enter a coupC in which Kate Castleton
sat. A lady emerged from a doorway and, seizing him by
the coat, said, in a low, strained voice, “ Pet, don’t you
think you had better come home?” “Your husband,
madam?” asked the singer, politely. “Yes.” The lady
said rather more than that, but it is not necessary to
repeat what she said. The singer smiled. “Do take him
away, please. He is a dreadful bore. 1 only let him see
me home because he said he would blow his brains out if
1 did not.”
Ada Campbell, the Australian Freethought lecturess,
was benefited by the Newark (N.J.) Freethinkers on
August 16, ere her departure for England, and on which
occasion she delivered a farewell lecture entitled “Good
and Evil, Vice and Virtue, Men and Women.”

Leitch’s dramatic adaptation of “ The Light that Failed,”
and has transferred to him the Australian rights.

Henry E. Abbey, in recognition of Goody’s services
during the Bernhardt season, a handsome gold matchbox,
two sides of which are inlaid with rich studded quartz.
Goodv suffers from one great regret in connection with
The Bernhardt’s visit—that on the occasion he was privi-
leged to take Sara B. to the Hawkesbury to show her
how many dozen bream could be caught at Woi Woi
in an hour the weather became so rough that she could
not fish at all, but had to go shooting.
Charles Wilmot stands a good chance of getting a music-
hall license for the New (London) Olympic. He will, in
point of fact, convert it into a smoking theatre, a thing
vigorously demanded in London.

Florence St. John, Teddy Lonnen, and Arthur Roberts
will be the stars.

When John Sheol Sullivan, the eminent actor, was
travelling overland to Adelaide, at one of the stations an
unassuming stranger entered his carriage. “’Ere,” said
John, “this blank carriage is reserved.” “Is it?” said
the stranger—" well, I’m coming in, anyway.” “ Are
you?” observed John—and a few seconds later the new-
comer's portmanteau went flying through the window.

There is no semblance of humour about the
very Christian female. There is very little
humour about the very Christian male
either for that matter, and the Christian
teetotaller, male or female, in all his or her
varieties, is as solemn as a crucified mule. The
following gaudy assertion, made by a woman at
the hen-convention show —alias the New South
Wales Women’s Christian Temperance Union—
the other day, is a case in point:
“ You see,” said the speaker, “ Moses stood in the same
relation to the Israelites as Sir Henry Parkes does to us.”
We have been looking up a few points about
Moses to see where the resemblance comes in,
and we find that eminent Hebrew was more
like Parkes than we used to imagine. He took
the Israelites out into the wilderness, and walked
them off their ancient Hebrew legs for 40 weaxy
years, in the effort to reach a place that any smart
pedestrian could have got to in 60 hours ; and
with all this walking he never arrived there
after all. He left his one and only
job unfinished, Parkes leaves all his
jobs unfinished, too, and he will cheer-
fully trot the Woman’s Suffrage League off
its limbs, and never get anywhere in just the
same faslxion. Moses broke all the command-
ments at once, which may or may not be
another case in point. The old Jew of the
Ax-abian desert is buried in an unknown
sepulchre, and no one knows where he lies ; in
other words, he hadn’t any Dead House ; and
Parkes also has lost his Dead House, in conse-
quence of which no one is likely to know
where he lies either. He was 120 years of age,
and had done no particular good for the last 40
years, and Parkes says he will live to be 120
also, and he never did any good at all. When
the Israelitish chief pegged out on Mount
Pisgah “ his eye,” saith Holy Writ,
“ was not dim nor his natural force
abated,” and probably the Women’s Tem-
perance Convention admires Parkes for
one of these reasons. Also Moses fixed up
twelve huge curses which were fired off on
Mount Ebal, and though Parkes curses on
level ground now by reason of his damaged leg,
which prevents him getting up to any great
elevation, yet for vigorous and original
anathema he is all there. Consequently, if the
Women’s Convention x-eally wants a Moses, it
might do worse than give the job to Parkes.
It seems probable that Balamaceda has
suicided, and cremated himself secretly.
Brewer Toohey, M.P. (N.S.W.), says he is
“prepared to vote for complete prohibition of
liquor-selling,” if such an achievement could be
attained, but he “ knows it is impossible.” It is
Golconda to a mousetrap that he’ll continue
“ knowing its impossibility ” till he becomes the
cynosure of a first-class wake.
The steel suspension-bridge being built by
the North Sydney Tramway and Investment
Co. to connect North Sydney to Willoughby will
stand 180 ft. above water-level, and is the third
largest of its kind in the world. At a dinner to
celebrate the swinging of the first cable, Mr. J.
R. Carey said he would be prepared to put down
£50,000 towards building a bridge between Sydney
and North Shore.
A N.S.W. country paper regrets to be
compelled to inform its readers that “our
esteemed friend and subscriber of many years
standing, Mr. , J.P., while engaged in
the performance of his farm-duties was severely
bitten on the hand by a breeding sow.” Then it
goes on to relate the nature of the wound, the
number of the stitches put in by “our respected
and talented friend, Dr. ,” and the style of
poultice used by the sufferer. What joy.!

Personal Items.
Five of the Judges of the N.S.W. Supreme
Court have their sons as Associates.
Remark erroneously attributed to David
Syme : “ The penny is mightier than the sword.”
John Dillon, so ’tis said, is to marry Miss
Emmett, of the U.S., grand-niece of Robert
Emmett.
Mr. Peter (ex-Judge) Faucett, M.L.C., is
the sole survivor of the N.S.W. Martin Ministry
of 1863.
An exchange says that Jimmy Tyson will
this season sell, from off his Queensland stations
alone, 90,000 head of cattle.
The octogenarian Sir William Manning,
is said to be seriously ill, as the result of an
abnormal growth on the neck.
One of the most prominent Sydney detec-
tives has just resigned because he found his
expenses to be a shilling a day more than his pay.
Millionaire James Tyson is credited with
being a woman-hater, but strangely enough most
of his business papers are in feminine handwriting,
Mr. John Deasy, Im Pay for West Mayo,
has married a Carlow girl named Tynan. His
histoi’ical Australian “mash,” curiously enough,
was named Drynan.
W. G. Grace this time gets £3OOO as his
“expenses” to play cricket in Australia. And
yet W. G. Grace is not a “professional,” but
counts in with the “Misters.”
There are five deaf men and twenty-five
bald heads in the N.S.W. Assembly. Schey, M.P.,
fearfully deaf, is also unfortunately threatened
with a complete loss of voice.
Bright’s disease is stated to be Judge
Foster’s malady. Having been refused leave, the
Judge, who is said to be of independent means,
may be expected to resign early.
London’sLordMayor—magnificent beadle!
—when banqueting Kaiser Wilhelm, asked the
reporters, who were near the Royal table, to
“ place their best-dressed men in front.”
Frank Miles, the English artist who used
to do all the pretty, weak, little sketches of pretty,
weak-looking women, has died at last—in the
lunatic asylum where he had long existed.
Hon. and Rev. Father Plunkett, the Bal-
larat Redemptionist priest who is now preaching
all over Australia, was formerly an officer in the
Fusilier Guards. He is uncle of Lord Fingal.
Cumberland Argus (Parramatta) gives
82 columns, question and answer, of Cyrus Edgar
Fuller’s insolvency, and promises a Second similar
dose. Cyrus happens to be the rival local editor.
The death of Sir John Steel, the Scotch
sculptor, immortalised by his statue of Sir Walter
Scott in Princes-street Gardens, Edinburgh, has
given the Sydney press a big show to mis-spell his
name.
Fitzgibbon intends to be something more
than a mere Chairman of the Melbourne Board of
Works. He has ordered an elegant throne whereon
to sit and consider the question of underground
sewage.
J. P. T. Caulfield, the Single-taxer, lu.B
just christened his first and Only son, “ Henry
George.” In the irony of fate, Baby Politician
Henry George Caulfield should grow tip to become
a big land-monopolist.
George Ash, M.P. (S.A.), late editor of
Narracoorte Herald, is studying for the law
and is articled to Hon. C. C. Kingston, M.P.
Ash has had a hankering for the law ever since his
big defence speech in his libel case.
A Mr. Wiggins was judging pigs the other
day at the Adelaide Agricultural Show, when a-
boar came up and took two immense bites out o"
him. He hadn’t given it the prize, and when the
outraged animal found itself passed over it rushed
him.
Alfred Deakin’s walking-costume oft pro-
claims the Tory-Radical, He doesn’t scruple to
wear a black silk bell-topper with a blue serge sac
suit. Garbed in these conflicting elements, Alfred
looks as though the flesh is willing, but the head
weak.
Tobacconist Eve, who lately unseated
Wheeler, M.P., on petition over careless scrutiny,
brought down the N.S.W.' Assembly in his
maiden utterance by stating that had he “been
up to snuff he wouldn't have been so long out in
the,cold.”

A Solomon Island nigger lately fired at
Captain Davies, of H.B.M.S. “Royalist,” who
instantly potted him in self-defence. Ere the
nig. died he was asked why he had fired on the
skipper. “Me tink him miss’nry,” replied the
simple savage.
Pastor Daniel Allen, of Sydney, just
deceased, was real grit, if extremely bigoted.
About a year ago, lie was surgically treated for
cancer in the lip. He refused to be chloroformed,
and never let a sound escape him during the
whole agonizing operation.
Should Mr. G. B. Simpson, Q.C., accept
the prospective N.S.W. Acting Supreme Court
judgeship, he will be the second District Court
Judge given a seat on the S.C. Bench—Mr.
Cheeke was the other. Mr. Simpson was Metro-
politan D.C. Judge in 1870.
General Booth says his father was specu-
lative, acquisitive, and wonderfully calculating,
without a, spark of religion ; “the General, there-
fore, attributes all his own good qualities to his
mother.” Just so, but all we know to date about
General Booth is that he is speculative, acquisitive
and wonderfully calculating.
Lord Rosslyn— one of the young noodles
caught in a gambling-hell with little Dudley and
others, a couple of years ago—is losing as much
as £22,oooover one “dead bird ”at Newmarket.
Bar Dudley and Rosslyn, every boy of the afore-
said crowd is already stone-broke.
It is whispered that in fixing up the late
Minister Sutherland’s (N.S.W.) “effects ” certain
dishonoured documents of monetary import to an
exalted Australian politician were found amongst
the papers. The point of the matter is that
£3OOO was voted to Minister Sutherland’s widow
by N.S.W. Parliament.
Mr. Dwyer Gray, chief proprietor of
Dublin Freeman’s Journal, is but a little over 21
years, and seemed a very sawney youth when in
Australia a year or so ago. On his reasons for
turning his journal against Parnell, some light
may be thrown by the fact that his mother is a
devout Catholic and is likely to enter a convent.
Miss Annie Barker, once widely-known as
“The Lady Stockbroker,” died in Melbourne
Hospital, the other day, aged 05. Unluckily she
succeeded to a legacy, 20 years ago, and went in
heavily for speculation on Melbourne mining-
exchange. At first she made a little pile, but
afterwards she lost all, and descended to a stall
in the Eastern Market, where she failed to hold
her own.
“Jawbone” Neild is introducing a Bill,
in the N.SAV . Assembly, for the Suppression of
V ice. This sort of thing makes us reflect on the
differences between different kinds of people, and
on the fact that some of them are a lot more
modest than others. It is true we don’t think
much of other people, but then we don't think
anything of ourselves. If we took it into our
head to suppress vice, we would begin by sawing
our own larynx. Go thou and do likewise.

Sir Henry Wrenfordsley left W.A. to
accept more money in Fiji, but as he took the
part of the weaker side in the row between the
planters and the natives, he was soon got rid of.
He next got a brief billet in Tasmania, followed
by a second one in Victoria; then he “otium'd”
for a spell, until ho contrived to be called by
(xovernor Robinson to return to W.A. as Acting-
Chief Justice. And now he is off to the land of
Yellow Jack —as Chief Justice of the West Indies
at £l5OO a year.
Charles Rasp was six years ago a boundary
rider over the country containing the great Broken
Hill Proprietary Mine, at a salary of “ thirty bob”
a week. He now receives in round figures about
£50,000 per annum, and, so fa’’ as science can
prognosticate, his shares will yield as much or
more yearly until the children of to-day are grey-
headed. Rasp stuck to the big mine from the day
he secured his first syndicate share for helping to
peg out the claim.
A certain rich Australian brewer, lately
staying at Southsea, England, one morning was
playing with, or teasing, a little dog belonging to
the hotel-landlord, when the animal turned round
and bit him. There was a great commotion, and
the victim, in mortal dread of hydrophobia, was
taken by Fred. Want to a chemist to have his hand
cauterised. Next morning (the bitee being all the
while in a blue funk) Fred, went to his room and
said, “ Old man, the dog is dead, and the vet. has
just made a post-mortem.” The brewer, intensely
excited, gasped out, “ Oh, tell me what did he die
of? was it rabies?” “No, old fellow,” said the
cheerful Fred., “it was D.T.”
The Hopkins-Searles (U.S.) affair is a
startler. Mrs. Hopkins, 73 years old, was cap-
tured by Searles, an impecunious paper-hanger,
aged 40. Under her management her first hus-
band’s colossal estate had accumulated to 73
millions of dollars— fourteen millions of pounds
sterling —and when she died four years after her
second marriage she left all her money to Searles,
ignoring every other person and thing under
Heaven—not even leaving enough to keep Hop-
kins’ tombstone in order. The lawyers are con-
spiring to burst the will, but it is cleanly and
clearly drawn, and the old woman seems to have
been as sane as Pilcher, Q. C.
William Steward, one of the claimants for
the Victorian reward to the first discoverers of
gold in Bendigo, died suddenly in Cqllingwood
(Melb.), on 17th inst., aged 67. He is said to
have started digging at what is now Golden
Square, Bendigo, in ’5l, in mateship with Chris,
Asquith, Jas. Graham, and Ben Bannister. He
leaves a widow to push on his claim.
Once in a way grovel meets its due reward.
The five-year-old King of Spain recently lured a
venerable military grandee into a damp place.
There was a sassiety “function” going on in the
grounds of the palace when his dear young
Majesty yelled for the old geeser aforesaid to
come and look at some flowers. His Majesty
waited until his loyal subject was bowing particu-
larly low and then turned the garden engine on
him. This is about the funniest thing accomplished
by a crowned head since Emperor Maximilian
got himself murdered, and can only be accounted
for by the extreme youth of the little joker.
There is a place in the Victorian Assembly
reserved for distinguished visitors, and there, just
out of quod, remote, unfriended, melancholy, slow,
and absolutely alone, sat little Jimmy Mirams the
other evening. Right opposite him was John
Nirnmo, and Mirams fixed him with his eye when
he first got in, and kept him fixed all the time.
But Nimmo never looked at him once. He gazed
at the floor with utter nonchalance—in fact,
Nimmo was never half so nonchalant in all his
life before. His dull, fish-like -eye said plainly
that there was no such man as Mirams, and that
Jimmy was hereby abolished, and that he was
beginning to feel convinced that he never had
existed at all.
Charles Sydney Dick Melbourne, a pro-
minent solicitor of Central Queensland, died the
other day at Rockhampton. His real surname
was Dick, but he called himself Melbourne for
exactly the same reason that the present writer
would call himself Bagdad if he felt that way.
U S. D. Melbourne was for many years about the
best lawyer in the city of perspiration, alligators,
and woe, till after a while there arose a small but
brilliant practitioner named Pattison, whose
underjaw stuck out like the firebox of a locomo-
tive, and whose head was just high enough to run
into Melbourne’s bread-basket, and the latter
seemed to get dispirited. His end was peace, and
all the leading citizens of Rockhampton helped to
carry his initials to the grave.
Just read this story, which is going the
rounds of the “good ” papers
A clerical tramp, one of the begging-letter Pecksniffs,
so often met with in London, called at Spurgeon’s house,
but refused to give his name. “ Say, if you please,” said
the tramp, with turned-up eyes, “a servant of Christ asks
for a few moments of his precious time.” The butler
came back immediately with the reply ; “Mr. Spurgeon
requests me to say that he is occupied'with your master.”
The pietists obviously think this a smart retort,
but to the sinner the paragraph is nauseous, not
to say revolting. The idea of a cross-grained, pig-
faced person like Spurgeon personally communing
with God, and sending his message to the other
worm by the butler on the loathsome occasion !
One Pecksniff collided with a still more disgusting
Pecksniff. That’s all.
Brewer Toohey, of the N.S.W. Legisla-
ture, says drink is authorised by the Scriptures.
That is no doubt the chief reason why “Jim”
sells it. Another reason is that grog-selling turns
James and brother John in, say, something
between £20,000 and £40,000 a year. Apropos, an
anecdote of John Toohey. When Pasteur’s dis
covery revolutionised fermentation-theories, the
brewer, to whom a copy of the great physiologist’s
book had been sent by a friend immediately after
its publication, knew no French : nor did he then
know anybody to whom he cared to entrust the
translating, so he went to work in his spare hours
and learnt the language. He learned it so effec-
tually that in a few months he had translated the
book, and begun to make £4OOO a year additional
out of his newly-acquired knowledge.
Old Geoff. Eagar’s scholarship appraised
by Parkes ! It was provocation enough to make
the old Liberal turn in his newly-made grave.
Geoffrey Eagar was not only a good English and
classical scholar, but a fair essayist and poet.
How unlike Parkes ! Eagar was a contributor
to the leading columns of the Monitor, Aus-
tralian, and Examiner ; and he possessed, as a
legacy, the only complete set extant of the
Southern Cross, which abounds with Deniehy’s
brilliant writings. Eagar’s suspension of Duncan,
collector of customs, was resented by Parkes; and
in defence of his old friend, perhaps also in
gratitude for publishing his “ early poems ” in
Duncan’s Weekly Register, in the early forties,
the “old parliamentary hand” resigned his first
portfolio.
Once in a way David Syme is guilty of
something resembling a joke. The other morning
he was standing outside the Age office, consider-
ing the profits how they grow, when his eye fell
upon a former slave of the staff, a dismissed
minion, whose face suggested a pertinent enquiry
as to how the young man was getting along in
the cold world. The ex-representative of the
Age, being rash and forgetful, hinted that he was
making a rapid fortune on vague and general
principles, whereupon David advised the budding
capitalist to step inside the old shop and see the
cashier on urgent business. David remembered
that this young friend had, during one of those
weak moments which overtake the strongest
intellects, contrived to borrow a parting trifle
from the firm on his 1.0. U. The perjured repro-
bate got away, however, leaving David and the
liability still outstanding.

Leaves little to deplore;
Of course, he’s found
Another golden shore.
If gold be thereabout,
He’s bound to pan it out.
Is War Deadly?
“Can anyone answer that ? It’s doubtful, very
doubtful.” War may not be healthy or con-
ducive to longevity, especially for the front
rank of the participants in the frolicsome pas-
time. We read in the big book how the re-
porters brought home records of hundreds of
thousands slain in a single battle ; but now,
with all our modern deadly weapons, smokeless
powder and noiseless guns, we fall far short of
the numbers slain in the scriptural history days.
We, however, make up for deficiency in this
respect by slaughtering thousands and hundreds
of thousands by living an artificial life. We
constantly struggle to live and obtain wealth,
we cultivate a special form of life to induce
disease, we drink alcohol and other stimulants,
and thus live in a continual condition of nervous
excitement. This enervates the whole system,
but so slowly and insidiously that we never
suspect the danger till some fatal disease attacks
some vital organ, and then the patient runs
panic-stricken, first to one doctor then to
another, in the agony of despair. People should
be wise in their generation, and beware of the
approach of the enemy, and ward off' his initial
attack. Remember that a continual languid
feeling, and sharp attacks of neuralgia at night,
with frequent or occasional headaches, or aching
limbs, sleeplessness, hot burning urine, with
constipation and loss of appetite, are each and
severally signs sent by Nature to warn you of
the physical disturbance, and that you" must
not neglect such symptoms, or your "case may
get beyond the power of medicine to cure. To
turn aside the disease, and render the system
impregnable to its ravages, a faithful and
regular treatment with Clements Tonic is
essential. This remedy never can fail to restore
healthy and normal action to every organ, as it
treats the disease, and not the symptoms. We
received from Alfred Godfrey, Esq., Solicitor,
391 Darling-street, Balmain, the following
letter: “Jan. 19th, ’9l. Dear Sir,—lt affords
me much pleasure to recommend Clements
Tonic to all persons whose systems require
bracing up. I have found it highly beneficial.
I don’t think it’s equal can be found.” Also
the following proof from Mrs. G. Wallis, 6(1
Ivy-street, Redfern, N.S.W. “ Dear Sir, —
Having suffered for nearly two years from
sciatica, and, being unable to obtain relief from
doctors, I was induced to use Clements Tonic.
I had small hopes of relief; but one large and
part of a small bottle completely cured me, and
that is now eight months since. The pain first
struck my left-side, extending to the back and
down the leg below the knee. At times the
pain was so severe that 1 could not rest day or
night. I became so very thin and weak that
my neighbours chaffed mo, and said there would
soon be a funeral; but, thanks to Clements
Tonic, I am completely cured. My daughter
also suffered severely from toothache and pains
across the eyes. She used Clements Tonic, and
is completely well ever since. I have also
recommended it to many people, all of whom,
praise it highly, and you may use this as you
like, and I shall be only too pleased to answer
any enquiries, trusting I may bring relief to
other sufferers. ” These are the kind of letters
we are continually receiving by every mail.
We only warn the public to be careful about
getting the genuine. There are imitations in
every chemist’s shop. See the signature of E-
Moore Clements on the wrappers and labels of
every bottle, and expose the swindlers who try
and put you oft' with an imitation.—Labor-
atories, Newtown, N.S.W.

The N.S.W. Parliament is a byword
amongst the Australias Even its square-jawed
Speaker is inoculated with a spirit of wanton
waywardness. According to Speaker Abbott, if
the Parliament decide in, say, January not to
raise additional revenue, it cannot reverse that
decision or even entertain the question again in
that session, though it last a twelvemonth, and
the State treasury be reduced to its last copper .
Tawell, the duly-hanged Quaker, who
founded the teetotal movement in Sydney, was
cited by John Haynes to the prohibitionists in
the N.S.W. Parliament, to show that women and
children have as much to fear from teetotallers as
from drinkers. Tawell knocked the heads on
many casks of rum and emptied the liquor into
Port Jackson, just ere plunging up to his neck in
a cold-blooded crime which convulsed the world.
Sir John Downer, Q.C., in the S.A.
Legislative Assembly:—
They must first of all agree on some principle as to
what gives the right to property. The only natural right
that there could be to any property was derived from its
being acquired by the actual industry of brain or hand or
an individual exercising his right 101116 free exercise ot
his body and mind. That was his first proposition,
that a man has no right to any properly except that
which he has created as the result of his ability and
energy.
Sir John’s Tory pals are trying to get him to
recant these revolutionary utterances.

Apropos of David Christie Murray s allega-
tions that Australians are drunken, statistics
show that of all the civilised countries only
Russia, Austria and the United States show a
lower consumption of spirits, and N.S.W. Govern-
ment Statist Coghlan points out that if the great
preponderance of males over females in Australia
is to be taken into account the figures will show
still more creditably for us. In Great Britain the
consumption of alcohol per head is o o i gallons,
while in Australia it is but 2'82.

A Vegetable Products Commission has
been tracking the Victorian cabbage to its lair for
seven long years, so Jimmy Munro is girding up
his loins for an effort to squelch that ancient
institution. The commission is one guinea per
sitting for each member, about the same as the
virtuous and economical James draws from his
various board-meetings. But whereas some of
Jimmy’s companies are addicted to winding them-
stives up within a reasonable time, the vegetable
products enquiry might draw on the public funds
for evermore, lienee the Victorian Premier’s dis-
satisfaction.
Parkes’ Cabinet, though it exists on the
sufferance of the Labour-party, jibs at the passing
of a Bill to compensate seamen injured through
the negligence or the avarice of shipowners in not
properly equipping their ships. Minister Gould
objects to the Bill leaving the Assembly, because
it is slightly “incomplete.” How badly would
this flimsy objection tell against all Parkes’
measures? Often and often Mr. Gould has gi t
the approval of the Assembly to a Bill on the
condition that it would be amended in some
respecter other in the Council. Why, then, this
new-born opposition ?
J. M. Toomoy, secretary of Young (N.S. W.)
branch of the Shearers’ Union, reports that everj -
thing is going splendidly under the new Conference
agreement; that non-union shearers and general
labourers are fairly rushing the Union for tickets;
that shearers are scarce in almost every part of
N.S.W. ; that on the Murrumbidgee and lower
part of the Lachlan there is room for fully 800
of them ; that the wonderful development of the
General Labourers’ Union has caused a rise in
wages from 15s. and 20s. to 255. and 30s. per week ;
that a plan has been mooted for forming an Acci-
dent and Funeral Fund in connection with the
two unions; and that the annual demonstration
will be held in November next.
The Bulletin loathes prohibition and the
pi’ohibitionists, but it likes logic. G. H. Reid
sees the case clearly—from his own point of view.
Liquor-prohibition, as applied to confirmed
bachelors, would be an outrage on liberty. Every
man who is a hopeless, virtuous bachelor has an
absolutely inalienable right to the management of
his own carcase, and must be permitted to stoke
it with whatever he .pleases whenever he pleases.
Anyone prepared to deny this proposition must
be counted in with the tyrants who want to com-
pulsorily foist their own peculiar spiritual
nostrums on to the souls of other citizens. It is
only when an over-drinking bachelor —which
G.H.R. isn't—proposes to get married—which
we hear G.HR. doesn’t—and hand down to his
progeny the alcoholically-induced transmissible
diseases of his organism, that the State has any
right to interfere.
There was a queer debate in the Victorian
Legislative Council last week over a Bill to enable
the sale of properties attached to St. James’
Anglican Cathedral, Melbourne. Behold in this
ease some of the mischievous sequelae of State aid
to religion. A big chunk of rich city property
was given, half-a-century ago, to St. James’.
The Church leased all the frontages (now im-
mensely valuable) to Collins-street and William-
street, for warehouses, and now wants to sell right
out, applying the money to alien purposes alto-
gether—nominally “Gospel propagation in the
bush.” The West Melbourne members objected
that if the freehold be given the money ought to
be expended in the evidently needful work of
gospelising West Melbourne, but that doesn t suit
the good old Church at all. One impudent logician
declares that the coin ought to go back where it
belongs—into the consolidated revenue.
The temper of JS.S. W. Parliament is
strongly averse to Griffith’s Federation scheme.
In the Council the Constitution will be amended
for a certainty ; in the Assembly, it is viewed
with undisguised hostility by a large majority,
but at present no one can gauge the intentions of
the opposing elements that constitute the majority.
The House, as a whole, may shelve the question,
or it may consent to re-cast the Bill. Several
members had determined to teat the feeling of the
House on a union of the provinces “under the
Crown.” But of this privilege they are now
deprived by the imprudent action of Republican
Rae, M.P., in giving notice for a distant date of a
specific motion in favour of a fedei’ation on Repub-
lican lines. If Parkes moves his resolutions, as
doubtless he will, antecedent to that date, the
Republicans in the House cannot raise the dis-
tinct issue, and will have to vote on the merits of
the Griffith scheme. Speaker Abbott is bound,
both by rule and by feeling, to prevent any general
reference to the coming Australian Republic : any
detailed discussion is prevented by the existence
of Rae’s motion on the notice-paper. What Rae
should have done was to table his motion as a
contingent amendment on Parkes’ resolutions.
Of the stupid drafting of Parkes’ Repre-
sentatlon of the People Bill, a few clauses will
give an insight. The secrecy of the ballot is
removed, for instance, by a provision requiring
the presiding officer to place on the ballot-paper
the number opposite the elector’s name on the
roll. Again, there was a clause enabling a
scrutineer or any two voters to call upon the
retuvning-ofticer to put a series of questions to
each intending voter. What a powerful weapon
to put in the hands of the capitalistic classes!
As the challenging would occupy at least five
minutes in each case, how many workers could
possibly vote in their leisure hour? The plan of
the capitalistic classes would be to poll in the
morning, and block the workers with these ques-
tions in the dinner-hour and evening. Fortun-
ately, the power has been placed solely in the
discretion of the scrutineers. No machinery was
originally provided to allow a dumb or deaf or
illiterate person to vote ; the defect has been
cured. A wager on the result of an election
renders the maker, man or woman, liable to a
penalty of £2O. No more new hats or dresses !
Labour Disputes and the way out—the Co-
operative Irrigation Colonies.

Society
The latest is that the Burdekins are making
a big splash, as such splashes go, in London. ITp
to date they have had the sense to avoid chronic-
ling their social achievements in the Morning
Post and the Court Journal, but “ they seem to
go everywhere,” writes our correspondent. Mrs.
Burdekin’s good looks and nice manner have done
a lot for the gilded Sydney. Luncheons en fci'inille
at Burdett-Coutts’; dinners sans ceremonie at
Devonshire House; smart supper-parties at the
Amphitryon, &c., &c., are part of their every-day
programme. The handsome Duchess of Bucking-
ham appears to have taken a violent fancy to the
ex-Mayoress of Sydney.
s* * *
“ Miss Burdekin,” continues the scribe
above-quoted, “ is very mheh admired, and indeed
she is an exceedingly pretty girl. There is great
trepidation in more than one quarter lest the
m irked attention which young Victor Cavendish
pays her may not —er —ripen into something
warmer. Victor is, as you are no doubt aware,
the heir-presumptive of the Duke of Devonshire,
and as such is, of course, looked upon as the
‘ parti ’ of the day.
* * *
“ Alreadyparty wire-pullersare on the look-
out for Burdekin, and are trying to discover what
side he will favour in politics. It seems to be
quite taken for granted that he will settle down in
England, buy a big estate and blossom into a
bloated aristocrat. My own private opinion is
that he will do so, and that Australia will know
him no more, except as a flying visitor. It is
very probable that the portals of either the
Carlton or Reform would respond to his ‘ open
sesame ’ were he inclined to seek a seat in Parlia-
ment. The Tories in particular would like to
pick up a few rich men who would open their
pockets and keep their mouths shut. Everybody
wonders who is running the Burdekins. They,
themselves, give no hint.
# # *
“ The moral of the whole business is—and
let intending visitors take it to heart—when you
come to England and wish to get among the real
haul ton , don’t brag of any swell acquaintances
you may have made; and, above all, don’t blow
about your money.”
# * *
Mr. and Mrs. Henniker Heaton arrive by
the Victoria next week. Henniker has made a
success in the Commons by never talking about
what he doesn’t understand. He has devoted
himself to the postal business, which he has at his
fingers’ ends, and he is as sure to carry penny
ocean postage as that the sun is shining.
* # *
Henniker aforesaid has earned the undying
gratitude of ladies by making the British Post-
office authorities telegraph “shan’t” as one word
# * *
The Bulletin suggests that Ministers of
Education should get the gentle art of easy suicide
taught in the Australian schools. It makes us
shiver to read of such ghastly fumbles as death by
arsenic.
# # *
It would appear that Griffiths, the mili-
tary officer who mysteriously disappeared on his
wedding-eve at Melbourne is not dead, but gone
before. His reason for clearing out appears to
have been that he was required to become a
proselyte to the religion of his fiancee. It is not
every man who, having to elect between his God
and his girl, gives preference to the former.
** ' #
The N.S.W. Parliament is seriously asked
by Minister Carruthers to allow a woman to
register the name of any man as the father of her
, illegitimate child without giving the man an
opportunity of seeing, or contradicting (or making
her prove) her statement.
* * *
Many persons will sympathise with a pro-
minent N.S.W. civil servant who has been
brought low through speculating on the advice of
confiding friends. When “he held it” he was
generous to a degree, as his big prizes to scientific
sport always proved.
# * #
Under J. B. (short for Jawbone) Neild’s
Suppression of Vice Bill any one, male or female,
who winks the other.eye in a public street incurs
a penalty of £lO or two months.
# * •
Jawbone’s former proposal that 12 months’
imprisonment (now altered to three years) should
carry divorce with it was cutting tilings rather
fine. It was perilously on the borderland of a
time when a married diner-out, who had managed
to get locked up on account of the salmon and
other things, might find himself a single man after
doing 24 hours “without the option.”
# * *
Pybus and Wateidiouse, recent absconders
from Sydney, are natives of Vandemonia. A few
years back Pybua lost an elder brother, a hand
some fellow, on the Derwent, almost within sight
ef the seaside home of Waterhouse pere.
Not long ago" the chief of a Government
department in one of the Australias reported a
subordinate for dismissal on the ground that he
was “an incurable invalid.” The ministerial head
of the department refused to adopt the recom-
mendation, and when the subordinate came to
hear what his chief had done he waited on the
latter and stated his intention of having his chief’s
name brought up in Parliament on the ground
that he was an incurable lunatic. Strangely
enough the chief almost immediately resigned his
position.
* * *
It is satisfactory to note that Longstaff,
the Melbourne Art Student, in Paris, has been
allowed to buy an excellent picture for Melbourne
National Gallery. This should certainly lead to
further drafts on the French art treasures, and
tothedecookneyfication of budding Australian art.

A Sydney man named Scott advertises
that he and his family will in future hyphenate
themselves as Payne-Scott. Great Scott!
# # *
The Bulletin softly sniggers at the pic-
ture of ex-Gov. Carington which is to join those
of the rest of the N.S.W. Governors at N.S.W.
Government House. It is just the sort of portrait
which an individual like Carington deserves.
* * *
There was quite a pretty row t’other day
at a southern home of vice-royalty over an excess
charge—as her ladyship maintained—of one
shilling in the price of a trumpeter-fish. Which
reminds us that the great bother between one of
the vice-regal man’s predecessors, Sir John
Franklih, and his Colonial Secretary—a trouble
which threw a shadow over the sturdy seaman’s
later years—had its rise in the cabbage from
Government House kitchen-garden ! Montague,
the Secretary, complained that Lady F. reduced
his quantity of cabbage; and madam answered by
stopping all allowances.
* * *
The story of the sufferings of the Mildura
stokers supplies a good deal of food for contem-
plation (this latter is a S.M. Herald phrase, that
journal being a heavy feeder and much addicted
to contemplating things). Among other things it
reminds us of a recipe for live roast duck which
we once came across in a black-letter cookery-
book. It set forth that the duck was to bo
plucked and fastened inside a circle of live coals,
and cold water put on the wretched fowl’s head
and breast to keep it up, till it was nicely roasted.
Then it was to be brought into the dining-room in
its last moments, and placed on the table artisti-
cally browned and howling among the gravy.
The Mildura stoker hadn’t any gravy, and he
was badly cooked, being burnt a good-deal on
one side and underdone on the other, and
when he was ready he was served up in the lee
scuppers instead of on a dish, but still there were
a good many points of resemblance between the
two animals. Also, he reminds us somewhat of
the so-called Fire King, who, in the days of our
childhdod, used to go into a hot oven, naked,
except as to a pair of non-conducting shoes, and
sit on a camp-stool alongside a raw leg of mutton
until the mutton was cooked. Then he would eat
a bit of meat to show that there was no deception,
and come out and realise the applause and the
gate-money. Barring the applause and the gate-
money and the asbestos shoes and the mutton,
the resemblance between the Fire King and the
Mildura stoker amounts to quite a surprising
coincidence.
# * *
Mrs. Taylor, the notorious Melbourne
abortionist, who the other day was sentenced to
be hanged, and is now “doing” 15 years, had
nearly been tried by an old acquaintance, Judge
Hickman Molesworth. She did appear before
him at Ballarat Assizes, but the defence were not
ready to go on, and so the case was postponed
and tried in Melbourne. When Mrs. T. first
appeared before the Courts—some 14 years ago—
she stood in the dock with a pretty young French-
woman named Monichon, in the old Supreme
Court. Purves defended Monichon, and Moles-
worth, Taylor. While the jury were “in,” Hick-
man discussed the probabilities of the verdict
with the reporters. “ Purves had the advantage
over me,” thoughtfully observed young Moles-
worth. “He is defending the pretty woman.”
However, both got off then; the pretty one
perhaps pulled the other one through.
Dear Bulletin, — I have just read Rolf
Boldrewood’s “Squatter’s Dream.” One of the
chief scenes is laid in Kaverina, and the author
sets his aboriginal characters “ensnaringof eels ”
in one of the local rivers (Vide cap. 111., page 34).
Now, there are no eels in that part of the country
—none in any of the waters west of the Dividing
Range.—Yours, H. J. McOooky. Cadia, Sept. 14.
# # *
“The Inventor” writes us thus from
Melbourne: —
Dear Bulletin, — The discussion which is taking place
in your columns re the inventorship of “ Wolseley ”
shearing-machine is becoming interesting and amusing—
interesting as showing how ready some people are to
take unto themselves credit for other people’s work, and
amusing inasmuch as the bogus claims of Howard and his
friends can be settled by an appeal to the patent-office,
where, I assure you, that neither Mr. Wolseley’s name nor
that of his man Howard will be found attached to this
machine. — The Inventor. Melbourne, Sept. 14.
Theßulletin begs to remark that the Patent-office
does not necessarily furnish the slightest evidence
as to who invented anything. One of the most in-
genious inventions we ever saw was patented in
the name of a certain Abraham Moses—who had
lent the inventor some £2 ss. or so, at 650 per
shent monthly, and foreclosed the mortgage on his
brains. Neither does the ownership of a house
show who built it, or the ownership of a new
country show who discovered it, and the possession
of hair doesn’t prove that the possessor grew it.
He may have bought it in a shop. “The In-
ventor ” doesn’t authorise us to give his name. If
he really invented the Wolseley-machine he ought
to stand up publicly and sav so out loud.
* * w
The War Cry. solemnly relates how the
hallelujah mother of the Salvation “ Receiving
House ” at Christchurch (Maoriland) discovered
the other day, that the potatoes had run out.
Therefore the members of the household were
ordered to go down on their knees and pray hax-d,
and while they were praying two sacks and one
small bag of murphies arrived suddenly. Tha
story ends there. Presumably the three bags
dashed furiously in at the garden-gate by them-
selves, for no mention is made of anybody arriv-
ing along with them. This ugly rush on the part
of the vegetables is described as a perfectly com-
mon incident in Salvation circles; the Army
simply prays when it is hungry, and food comes
tearing up the street immediately afterwards.
# * *
The “Australian” auxiliary squadron has
left Sydney for Melbourne, en route to “ the rest
of the Australian capitals.” The Admiral will
surely find it necessary to be in Melbourne at
Cup-time on urgent business.
* # *
The girl Powell, who unsuccessfully pro-
secuted Dr. Massey, of Wycheproof (Vic.), for
alleged criminal assault, has, it is satisfactory to
hear, been committed for perjury on very clear
prima-facie evidence. The hysterical - woman
question is one of the troublesome problems of the
age. The worst of it is that about 30 per cent, of
the_ women who bring false charges ruin
their man. To show the lengths to which
hysteria will bring a woman, there is the case of
the girl who fatally shot herself in an English
railway carriage the other day, and said a man
had done it after assaulting her. She lied with
her dying breath.
# #
An Englishman writes to a Yankee paper
from Freemantle, Western Australia :
I buy my tobacco here from a man who, on board a
ship, cooked for dinner the mate’s head that some of the
crew had cut off. I often have business with a master-
mariner whose crime was rolling a man about up and
down the deck in a cask with nails driven through it
until he died. My meat I buy from a wealthy butcher
who was also sent out for his country’s good. A wealthy
bookseller is also an old hand. A leading jeweller and
watchmaker is another. I touch my hat to a gentleman
as he drives to business in his carriage-and-pair, who was
sent here for life for scuttling ships, thereby losing crews
and ships. A celebrated and wealthy pearl-merchant and
storekeeper, with whom I am well acquainted, came here
for forging Russian bank-notes. Since I have been here I
have been in almost daily intercourse with two mur-
derers, one clever embezzler who was town supervisor
here and is now in durance vile, five or six forgers, and
thieves innumerable ; so, you see, I have seen a variety of
the convict profession.
# * #
A Perth (W.A.) pressman, detailed to
report a recent Mayoral ball, mixed up the order
of the supper procession (as it appeared on the
programme) with the names of the dancers of
the first set of quadrilles. And so it came to pass
that the West Australian recorded, as an
alleged fact, that Bishop Parry, in knickers and
gaiters, partnered with Miss Wrenfordsley, and
footed it agilely in the opening quadrille. Mrs.
Parry seems to have fairly “squirmed” with
indignation, and has written hotly to the Perth
press denying the insinuations made, and assert-
ing that “ the Bishop has never taken part in a
dance since taking Holy Orders.”
• * #
Mrs. Harrison Lee, lecturing in Melbourne
on Woman Suffrage, narrates two strange eases.
A Melbourne Chinaman, married to ye poor
Hibernianess, packed their infant, a few months
old, off to China —och, wirrasthroo ! A young
woman, in Melbourne, married a man out of pity,
because he was dying of consumption, and entirely
supported him, but he willed away their unborn
child to the care of strangers. However, this was
just as well. The child of a dying consumptive
would be no great “ catch.”
* # »
An esteemed female inhabitant died in
America, the other day, and next day the bereaved
husband took unto himself a new wife. Somehow
the inhabitants failed to approve of this, and on
the wedding night they banged tin-kettles and
tooted strange discords on cow-horns outside the
window of the happy pair. They tooted and
hissed and barked for two hours, and then sud
denly the window flew up, and the bride, in a
fluttering white garment, appeared, and spoke in
reproachful accents. “Gentlemen,” she said,
“ this is most unseemly. Are you not aware that
a funeral took place from this house this very
morning ? Have you no regard for the memory
of the dead, or for the feelings of a bereaved
husband? How dare you profane the house of
mourning?” The crowd hadn’t looked at the
matter in that light before, and it silently cleared
out.
* * *
The latest intellectual banquet at the
Austral Salon was a paper on “Letters and
Letter-writing,” by a young lady who is under-
stood to give a weekly specimen of her cor-
respondence in poor old Melbourne Punch. After
this we had better prepare for a James Fairfax
lecture on the Art of Journalism.

To wanton mashers’s wicked arts
No maiden coy shall yield,
If e’er the lawful age of “ tarts ”
Be left to Jawbone Neild ;
Her virtue from the fowlers’ snare

Has Jawbone never dreamt,

The age when they can tempt.

Dear Sirs, —I have been a great sufferer for oyer

received but little relief until I commenced to use

nine years, and can now do my work as well as
ever I could, and also enjoy my meals, which for
many years I could not. —I remain, yours thank-
fully ’ THOS. FISHLR

# * *
Captain Logan fairly broke down when the
Victorian Marine Board suspended his certificate
for a month for alleged reckless navigation on the
occasion when the Manaponri lately touched
ground near Cape Schanck. It was the captain s
first mistake or accident in 40 years—he has been
in the intercolonial trade for 35. Captain Logan
is a brave seaman, possessing the esteem and con-
fidence of all classes, and his proper pride and
sense of honour are in no small degree displayed
in the manner he takes to heart what by many
men would be considered a small trouble. People
generally think that fti consideration of his stain-
less career the Board might have returned his
certificate at once.
# * *
Not long since a nautical personage, em-
bellished with as much gold lace and brass buttons
as the average fire-brigade captain, obtained entree
to the house of a Sydney land-boom king, and on
the strength of his gorgeous get-up was consider-
ably “gold-topped.” A few days afterwards, the
land king’s daughters were in town and went on
board the potentate’s ship to see him, expecting to
find him pacing the quarter-deck with a telescope
under his arm. Encountering the captain they
told him they wanted to see Mr. . The
captain told them they would find him for'ard.
They went for’ard and found Mr. , who
happened to be the lamptrirnmer, scrubbing hen-
coops !
# * *
Government Analyst W. H. Hamlet, of
Sydney, the other day analysed some publicans’
“samples,” forwarded by the police from Taree
(N.S.W,), and reported ;—(1) Ingredient for
colouring rum—caramel, or burnt sugar; (2)
ingredient to put good head on rum—and, The
Bulletin presumes, also on the drinker thereof—
-4 per cent, oil of vitriol, 2 per cent, fusel oil,
1 per cent, creosote, 1 per cent, acetic acid, acetic
ether and oil of winter green; noxious and in-
jurious to health. “The samples called respec-
tively, rum, brandy, and whisky, are each of
them fictitious, and made from illicit spirit, with
various proportions of the colouring matter,
essences,” &c.
Sept. 18, 1891.

of the Sydney papers.

stated, is mentioned as Judge Foster’s locum
ttnens. or permanent successor. Acting (or p im-

Yet another death from chloroform in
Sydney. As there has now been a long list of
such deaths in the presence of clever and careful
surgeons, the conclusion is inevitable that Sydney
must buy some shockingly bad chloroform.
# # *
At a baby-farm in Sydney a child 2| years
old was recently discovered shut up alone in a
room into which a piece of a leg of mutton was
thrown for the youngster to gnaw. In another
house a girl was cleaning a chimney, and her
apparel consisted of a “ chaff-sack and a dirty
chemise.”
THANKFUL FOR SMALL MERCIES.
Two shearers camped in hollow logs, out West during heavy storm.
FIRST SHEARER (Joe) : “I say Bill, don’t you pity the soldier coves in them circus tents
to-night?”
SECOND SHEARER (Bill): “My oath! But I say, Joe, ain’t we struck it lucky to have
such a good camp?”
FIRST SHEARER (Joe) : “You bet!”
For some time, owing to Government
economy, Adelaide Supreme Court Law Library
lias been without a librarian. The Adelaide
lawyers, therefore, with the universal natural
instincts of their profession, have stolen nearly all
the books.

Coroner Woore, of Sydney, now graciously
allows police-officers to sit down in Court. Time
W as when no policeman of any grade dare be
seated in that magnate’s judicial presence.
* * *
Influenza has been ravaging the “repre-
sentative” classes of Melbourne. The Bar was
stricken down every here and there ; the Hope-
touns, both lord and lady, had to be laid in their
little beds and dosed with quinine, after which tiie
Victorian naval officers were vanquished, all along
the line, by their Russian foe.
# * *
J. F. Hogan writes the article “Mel-
bourne ” in new vol. of Chambers’ Cyclopaedia,
just out. “ Bourke-street corresponds to the
London Strand, but is three times as wide and
four times as long.” Rather misleading this,
James. Is Bourke-street four Strands ? Childers
(merely because he happens to have turned
Londoner) is credited with Melbourne University,
and there isn’t a word of Sir Redmond. Ormond
and Sir Salmon Wilson are the only other men men-
tioned in any way—they and Childers are Mel-
bourne’s three productions. St. Parchrick’s, of
course, gets due glory. The Anglican Cathedral
(St. Paul’s) is “hemmed in by clustering ware-
houses ;” well, not exactly, Jimmy. He puts in
both Pentridge and Coburg as suburbs, on his
map.
A man who forcibly kissed two girls in
St. Patrick’s Cathedral, Melb., and got two
months for it, insists that the bliss was worth the
trouble. The girls, charmed with his gallantry,
now beg him off.

Evidently these particulars about Eliza’s com-
plexion and age are put in so that she may be able
to identify herself if she should happen to meet.
Otherwise she might walk right past, and not
know that it was she. The present writer has
been had that way himself. He once threw him-
self down two flights of stairs under the impres-
sion that he was another man altogether, and
only recognised himself by his horrid screams at
the bottom.
« * •
There is a man going round Tasmania in a
mysterious way selling obscene literature at a high
figure, and making a large profit thereon. Each
book is tied up with string so that the buyer can’t
open it until after purchasing, but the name of
some horribly dank and foul publication is on the
cover. Therefore the large, unctuous deacon buys
it in an obscure corner and goes away to read it
secretly under a gum-tree, and when he opens it
he finds nothing inside but a little girl’s Sunday-
school story, with a harmless moral. There is
hardly a gum-tree in all Tasmania now which
hasn’t some old, clean-shaved Christian cursing
under it, and kicking off the bark to a height of
eight feet or more, in his rage. And nobody can
sue the hawker for selling immoral literature, for
there isn’t enough immorality in his literature to
disturb your grandmother with; neither can he
be charged with false pretences, for no one cares
to swear that he was swindled, because the book
he bought ought to have been offensive, and
wasn’t. It is a great and profitable game.
* * *
There is one large factory in Melbourne
where none but red-headed people are employed.
The proprietor has a head like the glare of a
danger-signal at midnight, and every man, woman
and child on the premises is thatched with Ver-
million. The place is watched over at night by a
red-headed watchman, and a red-headed con-
tractor removes the ashes out of the back-yard.
Even the dog is the reddest dog that could be
obtained for love or money.
# # #
A strange disease has broken out of late
years in the lonely, storm-beaten island of St.
Kilda, off the coast of Scotland, which carries oft
80 per cent, of the children within a few days
after birth. St. Kilda is about the saddest and
most forgotten desolation on earth, and for
centuries the scanty population has married and
intermarried, after the fashion of European royal
families, until every man is his own uncle, and
his own cousin and second cousin, and everybody
is half-brother or something to the entire com-
munity. And now the game has got to be played
out, and collapse and idiocy are the natural con-
sequences.
# # *
Dr. Diamond Beaney threw away the finest
chance he ever had of advertising himself to the
whole world, and for ever and ever. Why didn’t
he reserve the site on which “Cromwell House”
now stands, and erect thereon a god (Bacchus) like
statue of himself in brass, enclose it with an ex-
pensive iron railing, devising the rest of his
wealth to the Executors’ Co. for the benefit of the
charities of Melbourne, so long as the statue
was kept in thorough repair ?
* # #
The Emperor of Germany has issued an
order that no Christian names “containing a
political allusion ” are to be registered in Prussia.
Which reminds us that Gov. Jersey had better make
a decree to prevent enthusiastic mothers from
christening their babies ’Enery or ’Enrietta Parkes.
We haven’t heard of any mothers doing so, up to
now, but there’s no knowing when a national
mania of that description might set in.
* * *
A Melbourne weekly, Life, ceased to breathe
the other week. The paper was born with good
intentions, and a ricketty constitution. It had
some brains, but no backbone, for a time. When
the financial backbone was introduced the last
remnant of brain departed from the struggling
periodical, and it degenerated into the dullest
darned insult overissued. It’s death was the one
funny thing that it had been guilty of since a new
management began to spend other people’s money
in “ pushing ” it along.
TO OUR BEST GIRL IN ENGLAND.
I love thee, darling, with a love intense,
1 loved thee well in England’s frigid clime;
I love thee here, where wild mosquitoes prance,
I mix thee up with scenery sublime.
For thee the kangaroos shall madly dance
(I’ll wrench thee from the land of frost and rime).
The alligator’s crocodilian chants
In ichthyosaurian tropes of ponderous rhyme
Shall bid “ Australia ” onwardly “ advance ”
To happy make thy glorious coming time
Some day perchance!
Then shall the savage throw his boomerang
High in the air his frenzy to express;
The dingo yelp, while bucks the wild mustang
To greet 'thee in thy queenly loveliness.
The bullock-bells upon the ranges clang
(Be sure you wear a pretty muslin dress).
The eucalypti branches proudly hang,
The measure of thy beauty to confess ;
The ’possum and the sweet ourang-outang
Shall climb the trees to worship thee, I guess—
While joy-bells bang !
The punciana’s gorgeous ruby hue,
The wallaby’s sweet song, the mopoke’s hoot,
Shall all unite in homage unto you ;
But far beyond, the rapturous bandicoot
Shall startle nature with a wild halloo
To all the South thy qualities to moot.
The platypus shall join the cockatoo,
The wattles throb with pleasure to each root;
And in thine honor every scenic view
Shall be presented in its Sunday suit —-
In Woolloomooloo!
Haste to the land of warrigal, of frog,
Of stringy bark, of kurrajong, of creek,
Oi thirstv drought, of flood, of native dog,
Of platypus (that strange mammalian freak);
Of syndicate, of fern, of cedar log,
Of pelican's exaggerated beak,,
Of strikes, of sport, of pugilistic slog.
List to thy lover’s melancholy shriek !
Forsake the land of Socialist and fog
And book for New South Wales next Tuesday
week —
Thy love to seek.
J. H. KNOWLES.
What saith the Church ? Ah, here the smirch is,
One has to ask—What say the Churches ?
The Greek Church says its holy priest
Must be the hub. of one at least.
No, that's not what we mean, we own,
ihist be the hub. of one alone.
The R.C. Church doth marriage shun,
An R.C. priest’s the hub. of none.
The English Church, with freer spike,
Lets clergy do just as they like.
Then, what excuse has Bishop Smith
To thus discourage marriage ? sith
St. Paul himself would have us learn
We’re better wed than we should burn.
When parsons must keep celibate,
The English Church’s emasculate.
FATHER NEILD OR FATHER PARKES?
(A N.S.W. PARLIAMENTARY LAY.)
All along for years on years unwelcome babies
came and went, #
And humanity sat silent in a silent Parliament.
Mothers, stricken with the sudden penalty of
wayward love,
Posted back, first chance they got, the pi’esents
sent them from above.
Other mothers, crushed and broken in subsistence
ceaseless fight,
And obliged themselves to starve or put their
burdens out of sight,
Gave the scanty coins they mustered in their
narrow, cheerless lot .
For a refuge for their offspring in an alien lap or
cot.
So a trade or a profession with a keen financial
aim . ,
Grew to ponderous proportions on necessity ana
shame.
For a sum in hand, or for instalments paid at
stated times
Need could end its troubles, Passion end the
sequels of its crimes.
In the trade the babe was entered as if taken in to
nurse,
But instead of cot or cradle it was rocked off in a
hearse,
Still the Parliament was silent till, the other
night, arose
Jawbone to place the woes of babes before lus
friends and foes.
With such truth and sympathetic tones he dwelt
upon his theme
That the House, with small corrections, ratified
his healing scheme.
But as soon as Parkes discovered that another
might go down
As the father and the saviour of the babies of the
town,
All his hair stood up protesting, and he suddenly
made known
That he had a scheme which laid out Neild and
left the field his own.
Out the scheme came; and the fight is now between
the rival guns,
Which shall figure—Neild or Parkes—as father of
the little ones ?
In the meantime while the rivals fume and glare
and jaw and curse,
Little lost ones keep on going—rocked off in the
same old hearse.

«A GOLDEN SHANTY.
TT IS INCONVENIENT,
UNPROFITABLE,
AND PARTICULARLY FOOLISH
To buy rubbish of an} 7 sort. If the best article of
its class cannot be obtained in one place it can in
another, and it is always bad policy to be bluffed
into buying the second best of anything, just for
the sake of effecting a temporary saving of either
time or money. For the saving
IS ONLY TEMPORARY,
and regrets, like curses, come home to roost.
This advice has a good, all round, general ap-
plication, but it is rarely heeded because it is so
cheap and common. The foolish housewife
listens to it and smiles, but vaunteth to herself
meanwhile her superior wisdom, while the wise one
closes one eye in silent meditation, and scores a
notch upon the handle of the broom or the head
of her nearest offspring, whereby she may after-
wards refresh her memory
AND PROFIT BY IT.
In no sense is this well-seasoned hint of so much
value as in its application to the purchase of oil
for domestic use, and in the best interests of every
consumer this should be cut out and pasted in the
hat of the head of every household. Never buy
inferior oil until you have discovered an insurance
company that will
TAKE ALL RISKS.
And even then it would be an unwise thing to do,
because as the time, place, and probabilities of
the risk are uncertain, the premium must be
abnormally high. Higher even than the wreckage
might be expected to go, and the contemplation
of that alone should be sufficient to impress the
merits of Luxene, the new illuminant, on the
minds of all Christian peoples.
LUXENE HAS NO SMELL,
GIVES A BEAUTIFUL LIGHT,
IS VERY ECONOMICAL,
AND UNQUESTIONABLY SAFE.
Try it, and be for ever grateful for the advice,
which is as disinterested as it is sound.
LOST TREASURE.
Mr. Win. Morgan, Chairman of the Pnkekohe
(N.Z.) East Highway Board, writes as follows;
“During harvesting operations in January last
one of my boys had the misfortune to lose his
Waterbury, but where he had no idea. He
searched the paddock, but could not discover the
lost treasure. A few days ago, however, one of
our neighbours picked it up, wound it, and it
started to go at once, although it had been lying
on the ground exposed to the weather for two
months. Surely such a fact is worthy of record
for the popular Waterbury.”
