The following suggestions are not designed to
induce the public to attempt the duties of the
regular surgeon, but merely to place the reader in
possession of a means of treatment of the minor
accidents occurring daily in the household. No
family can be assured against accidents; the
broken bone, the dislocated joint, the severed
artery, these are misfortunes that may possibly
happen to anyone; and we scarcely need to add
that they demand the instant attention of the
most skilful surgeon within reach. But by house-
hold accidents we mean those which are constantly
occurring every day, in nearly every family, and
which,’while not always dangerous in themselves,
are exceedingly annoying. Burns, bruises, cuts,
scalds, sprains, &c., are principal among these
troublesome and annoying occurrences, and
demand immediate treatment with the best means
at hand. In the kitchen, the dining hall, the
nursery, and the sitting room they are liable to
happen, and instead of fear and alarm at the
sight of the cut or smashed finger, or burned or
bruised arm, or scalded surface, a cool and quiet
manner should be assumed, and after washing
away the blood (if required), the injured parts
should be dressed with that most valuable remedy
—St. Jacobs Oil. Its especial worth for the
injuries named, and others of a similar nature,vis
not based upon mere claim and assertion ; but its
constant use for a long term of years has given it a
reputation which is always increasing. Its sur-
prisingly quick relief, its cleansing properties, its
tendency to quickly remove all inflammation, and
its wonderful efficacy in the above, as well as in
all muscular and other pains, such as rheumatism,
neuralgia, toothache, headache, stiffness of the
limbs, joints, &c., these qualities render St. Jacobs
Oil pre-eminently the best external remedy before
the people, which claim is fully substantiated by
the class of strong testimony voluntarily accorded
it by representatives of every class and station in
life.

A Melbourne newspaper, which ought to
know better, refers to Deniehy’s sketch, “How I
became Attorney-General of Barataria,” as the
work of Sir James Martin. There was never the
least pretension put forth by Martin or Forster
(to whom, also, the authorship has been ascribed)
to the paternity of that brilliant piece of satire;
and Deniehy was recognised as the author in
London literary and legal circles in the early
sixties. Dailey introduced Deniehy to G. B.
Barton,on the last-named’s return from England,
and Barton, as related by Deniehy himself in a
letter to his wife, delighted the little man by an
account of the impression made by the sketch on
members of the Temple.
The latest Victorian wreck, that of the
Fiji, occurred only 10 miles from that of the Loch
Ard, in 1878 —the latter one of the most unlucky
ships that ever sailed. On her first voyage she
came into Hobson’s Bay with two other ships,
the John Kerr|[and the Can.b.idgc shire, all three
dismasted. Next voyage, the Loch Ard was all
but lost on the Otway leeshore, hanging by her
anchors, through a fearful storm all night, and the
third voyage she was wrecked, when young Tom
Pearce, a midshipman, saved a passenger, Miss
Eva Carmichael, whereupon all Australia gushed,
though the lady weighed 12st. Capt. Robertson,
leaving the Loch Ard, perished with a number of
other captains in a Calcutta epidemic.
Scarcely has one appalling disaster on
Victorian waters been placed on record than
another of a no less awful character is announced.
The victims of the Gambier-Easby colli Jon are
hardly laid in their graves (where such has been
possible) when we have another shock in regard
to the ill-fated Fiji. It seems that this sea-
travelling is getting about as unsafe as a Yankee
railway, and if things go on as they are doing the
life-insurance people will refuse to take risks over
marine accidents at all. There are some people
who delight in “ being prepared,” as they call it,
by having their lives insured. They know then
that when they kick the slop-receptacle their
relicts will be in a position to set up some other
poor devil and start a fresh family upon the pro-
ceeds of their misfortune. Some otheis, however,
take a less happy view of the case, and prefer to
have the “stuff” in their pockets and take the
risk, with the necessary precautions for safety.
And, as a rule, they’re just as happy as the others,
and enjoy life, too. But these people are sensible
in one way—they always get even with the risk as
much as they can. So in regard to these ship
wrecks you generally find that the Insurance-
companies are struck by the poor wretches who
go down, but the others are more frequently
the ones who have nothing to gain for
themselves or their families by going under,
and they swim ashore. They nearly all swim—
these people. And after all don’t they insure
themselves in the best way? We gave an instance
last week of a man who saved himself from the
Easby through having learned to swim at the
Sydney Natatorium in Pitt-street, and it would
be an interesting thing to know how many others
were in the same category. In any case, this
institution has turned out a power of swimmers,
and you can’t tell how many others there were on
the Fiji and the Gambier who may attribute their
present existence to the fact that they learned the
natatory art at the Natatorium. As we have
previously pointed out, the baths there are very
fine, and now that the warm weather is creeping
on they ai-e becoming quite crowded again. For
the benefit of those who have never attended, we
may say that the Natatorium is in Pitt-street
near the Haymarket, and that the Hotel and
Baths form one of the most attractive sights of
the locality.

Work and Wages.
The Bulletin learns with regret that Judge
Foster’s illness is not only serious, but is
likely to amount to a permanent disablement.
During his three or four years’ service on the
Supreme Court bench (New South Wales) —he
was appointed in February, 1888—his Honor
has shown himself to be a conscientious, upright
and painstaking servant of the public. We
make this acknowledgment without departing
from the opinion we have always held, that he
is seriously lacking in the qualifications that
ought to be found in the holder of high magis-
terial office. We are altogether opposed to the
principle which regulates the selection of
Supreme Court Judges in New South Wales,
and more or less in every Australian province.
It is a vicious rule which bestows upon
the Attorney-General, for the time being,
a sort of prescriptive claim to any judge-
ship which may become vacant during his
term of office. ” Rumour goes that if Judge
Foster retires, his place will be taken by
Attorney-General Simpson. If Mr. Simpson is
the best man for the position, we have no objec-
tion. The fact that he is a colleague of Sir Henry
Parkes doesn’t prejudice him in our eyes as a
candidate for a judgeship. But urn insist that
that fact has no right to be treated as giving
him a claim to the appointment. It has no
right to be even taken into account as an
element in the question. If Mr. G. B. Simpson
has displayed judicial qualifications which point
him out as the fittest available man for the
post, and if his health is good enough to war-
rant the expectation that he will do fifteen
years’ good -work ere claiming his pension, then
he is entitled to it. But not otherwise.
* * *
There is another consideration that enters
into the case, one that derives special emphasis
from Mr. Justice Foster’s illness now. The
New South Wales Supreme Court Judges are
appointed at a salary of £2600 a-year, with an
annual pension of seven-tenths of that amount
on retirement, after fifteen years service, or on
permanent disability or infirmity. In view of
the fact that this pension is a tax on the public,
the public is entitled to some assurance at the
time of his nomination that he is not likely to
become a pensioner until he has done a fair
amount of service. A satisfactory medical
certificate should be an indispensable condition
of any man’s appointment to a post to which a
pension is attached. We sincerely trust, for
Judge Foster’s sake, that his indisposition has
been over-rated, and that a few months rest
will restore him to health and enable him to
resume his duties. But if he should un-
fortunately be compelled to retire, his account
with the public of New South Wales will stand
this way Credit; Three-and-a-half years’
work. Debit: £9lOO cash, and a pension of
£lB2O for life. Such a balance-sheet must be
as unsatisfactory to a man of Judge loster s
strict conscientiousness, as it most certainly is
to the tax-paying community.
* * *
Why should young men not be appointed to
the Bench ? The duties discharged by a Supreme
Court Judge require not only a knowledge of
the law—they require a clear, vigorous intellect
and a sound physical constitution. Mental
exhaustion and physical fatigue on the Bench
infallibly mean injustice to suitors. If a level-
headed man in the prime of life, with an
active intelligence, is not competent to acquit
himself creditably as a Judge after, say, 10 years
constant practice at the Bar, he probably never
will be. So far as difficult points of law are con-
cerned, as a rule both sides of the question are
put before him fully by the contending parties.
If he has any serious doubt upon the matter, he
can consult his brother Judges. That is done
now by the present Judges every day. As
proof that young men can perform judicial
functions satisfactorily, we have only to look at
the District Court. No one will pretend that
the best men there are the oldest. Physical
and intellectual sanity count for infinitely more
than long experience. And, speaking of the
District Court, why should not men who have
proved their mettle there be raised to the bench
of the higher court? What is the matter
with a man like District Court Judge
Backhouse that when a Supreme Court
Judge is wanted he should be passed
over, and the billet be given to some elderly
gentleman, who is getting too lazy for the
active duties of his profession as an advocate ?
The Bulletin is dead against the time-honoured
principle of keeping judgeships as havens of
dignified retirement for men who have out-
grown their capacity for hard work. Time-
honoured principles are generally weatherworn
and unseaworthy. When the public offer a
good salary and a pension for the performance
of given duties, their object is not to bestow
charity, but to get work done; and they are
entitled to get men fit to do the work.

About the biggest and easiest-got haul
made by a Chinaman on the Palmer, was made
from Jack M'Lean, now a squatter on the
Mitchell River. In the early days McLean was
butchering at Limestone, and the money came in
at such a rate as to necessitate frequent trips to
the bank at Maytown. On one occasion he
packed something over seven hundred ounces of
gold into a valise, which he put on a led horse.
After riding some miles he chanced to look
behind him, and, to his horror, discovered that
the valise was missing. He didn’t wait to swap
knives or swear, but started on the back track at
a Melbourne Cup rate. After riding some three
or four miles he found the valise all safe and
sound, but the gold had gone! The road swarmed
with Chinamen, and all McLean could get out of
them was “no savee.” It was afterwards ascer-
tained, beyond a doubt, that a Chinaman found
the valise soon after McLean had passed, took out
the gold and sailed for China by the first vessel
from Cooktown. Jack has ever since, and ever
will be, hopelessly soured against the Chinkie
race.

The late “Dick” Thatcher used to tel 1
this yarn. A Sydney man once gave a garden-
party at his suburban residence which was at-
tended by all the bon ton. Just as the last of the
guests were arriving a frowsy, downheeled loafer
ambled up the gravel walk and, eyeingthe company
with a deprecating look, sidled up to the host who
was on the verandah. Removing his battered
tile and making a low bow he asked : “Would
you like to hear a d d good comic song I
“No,” said the astonished host, “ what right have
you to come here? Don’t you see this ;s a private
party.” “Oh, that’s all right, guv’nor,’ replied
the dead-beat, “if you wouldn't like a good comic
song’ perhaps 3011 would like a good old step-
dance?” “No,” said the indignant host, ‘damn
you and your comic songs and dances I have
told you alreadv that you have no right to come
here,” and he took hold of the sundowner to put
him out. The guests crowded up to see what was
the matter, and the loafer turning round to them
said; “Ladies and Gentlemen, I’ve come here
thinking I could amuse some of you, but it appears
the boss don’t want no comic songs nor step-
dances. Well would any of you like to see the
DEVIL ? ” There was a pause, and then a demure
girl said timidly, “Yes, I would.” “Well, go to
h 1 then,” said the frowsy one, as he sped
down the gravel walk and nearly broke himself in
two getting through the gate.
At Manchester (Eng.) the other day,
James Healy was charged with bigamy, and
defended himself on the plea that his first wife
had disappeared for eight years, in consequence
of which unexpected scarceness on her part he had
set her down as a deceased female, and had married
again. Then the Judge informed Healy that if he
could swear to that circumstance and produce any
evidence to support his statement he could be
found not guilty, and Healy undertook to swear
and produce the evidence, and started in a
blindly confident way for the witness-box. He
was hurriedly shunted back, and told that as a
prisoner he couldn’t go into the witness-box.
Thereupon he wanted to swear in the dock instead,
but he was informed that he couldn’t swear in the
dock—it wasn’t allowed. Then he asked the Judge
where he could swear and give his evidence, and
was informed in reply that he couldn’t swear or
give his evidence anywhere; and when he
demanded why the plaintiff had been heard if the
defendant couldn’t be heard also, it was explained
to him that the British Constitution was arranged
that way. He got excited at this, and enquired
if the court called that justice, and the court said
it didn’t know, and sent Healy up for nine months.
And as the policeman hauled him away the J udge
stated in a consoling way that perhaps the Law
would be altered shortly, and the prisoner res-
ponded as he vanished into the regions below that
it would be darned little use to him if it was.

It is said that the German Emperor suffers
such intense agony at times from his chronic ear-
affection that he has to be largely dosed with
morphia besides having to submit to severe local
treatment. Finding that the illustrious sufferer
was beginning to weaken under this treatment,
his doctor allowed him a certain quantity of
Bavarian beer, which had been interdicted for
some time. His Majesty, who it is said is some-
what partial to this tipple, took advantage of the
permission to indulge in it to such an extent that
something approaching D.T. was the result. He
—so ’tis averred —dressed himself' in British
Admiral’s costume, held a religions service
at 1 a.m., and then went on the bridge of the
Hohenzollern, and insisted on navigating the
ship himself, to the imminent danger of the vessel
and all on board. Feeling the risk, a young officer
presumed to warn His Majesty, whereupon
Wilhelm boxed his ears. The officer went im-
mediately to his cabin and shot himself dead.
Continuing his vagaries on the bridge the Emperor
at last became giddy, or something, and came a
heavy cropper on to the deck, injuring himself so
much that he was not for some time able to go
about at all. This, says the Agence Libre, is the
“slight accident” which the inspired press has
minimised.
Wragge, Queensland meteorologist and
weather-prophet, when he is at home is one of the
sights of Brisbane. He is an absent-minded
skeleton in a slop suit, and always looks as if he
had mislaid himself and couldn’t remember where
he did it. He is pretty safe around Brisbane for
somebody always finds him and takes him home,
but when he visits Sydney he is generally accom-
panied by a small, Hebraic-looking gentleman
whom he calls his “chum,” and this individual
takes care of him. When Wragge stops right in
front of a tram-car to calculate the perihelion of a
comet, or hails an omnibus and then forgets to get
in because he wants to make an estimate of the
probable weather in the next 24 hours, the
“ chum ” seizes him by the back of the pants—he
can’t reach any higher—and dashes him into a
place of safety. Also he has to fish the naked
astronomer out of baths, for Wragge will stand
for four hours under the shower trying to arrive
at the density of Jupiter, or he will camp with
one leg on a railway platform and the other in an
express train that is two hours overdue and howl-
ing to get away, and there become entirely
oblivious. He is a profoundly learned man with
odd boots, and if he were on his way to see an
eclipse he would run over a Governor and tread
him into the mud and never turn a hair.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Humphrey Oxenham laid £l7OO to £5OO on
Marvel in the Craven Plate, and then “ played it up ” on
his own horse Hero, in the next race. As that also came
off the day must have been a good one for him.
* * *
It is said Blackham will not go to England
unless with a representative team.. Yet people used to
say that Blackham was so “shook” on a European trip
that he would travel home with a team of blackfellowa.
* • •
Without a doubt the field for the Metropolitan
this year was the worst that ever faced a starter for an
event of such importance, and, Highborn and Sunshine
taken away, there was not a horse in it good enough to
win an ordinary handicap. Joe Barton had next thing to
a certainty for Muriel had he elected to start her, and,
perhaps, after the Melbourne Cup he will be of opinion
that a bird in hand is worth a flock in the bush.

to 2 to 1, and many astute judges consider the son of

duced.

# # *
The recent performances of Hero, the aged son
of The Drummer and Camelia, make one wonder how a
horse of his calibre could have reached such an age with-
out earning a winning bracket. As a matter of fact, Hero
was an early foal and although he figures as an aged horse
in the Stud Book, he is in reality only six years old.
When a three-year-old he was cast in his box and was
thrown out of work, but he hears no lasting memento of
the mishap, barring a blemish on a hind leg.
# # »
The Olympic Club, Larry Foley’s show, has
cabled an offer of £2OOO for a finish fight between Prit-
chard and Fitzsimmons. Britisher Pritchard has just got
away with the big duffer Jem Smith, but that is not much
of a performance to gauge his merits as a fighter by.
Fitzsimmons has height and cleverness on his side, and it
remains to be seen who has the hitting power. Should
the pair meet, and Fitzsimmons be still leading his old
steady and abstemious life, he will need a tremendous lot
of thrashing.

The Yanks still swear by Sir Modred, two of

Hasbronck and Gold Dollar. Two days after that another,
Sir Matthew, won a stake at Jerome Park worth over
£7OO. Tournament, the Sir Modred colt which last year
won nearly £12,000 in stakes, has failed this year.

The Melbourne Cricket Association has
tackled Mr. Victor Cohen about promoting a Cricket
Council and then going outside it in the picking of a team
to go to England next season. V.C. explains that the
Cricket Council is not yet formed. Should it be consti-
tuted in time, the picking of the team will be left to it,
hut, failing its formation, it is proposed to pick five
“ certainties,” and let them choose the remainder of the
team.
# # #
The Queensland cricketer, Conningham, who Is
likely to go to England, has a batting average of 65 runs,
and a bowling average of 5 runs per wicket. He is a
level-headed, left-handed bowler, much after Ferris’s
style. He has shown his quality both in N.S. Wales and
in' Victoria, as in Sydney he scored 41 and in Melbourne
50 not out.

A benefit to Mrs. Johnson, mother of Alec.
Johnson, a lately-deceased reporter, was given by the
swimmers of Sydney per favour of Sydney Bathing Co. at
the Natatorium on Thursday night last. Several big per-
formances were done. Young Gormley, son of the Mur-
rumbidgee M.P., beat the English amateur record for
220yds.—2min. 51 l-ssec.—by the odd six-fifths, and won
the 220yds. handicap from scratch. Miss M. Nugent
swam over for the Ladies’ Race, and W. Wiley won the
distance dive with 65 yards. About 1000 people were
present.

Mr. “Billy” Macpherson, the champion ama-
teur, gave another wonderful performance at Sydney
Grammar School sports on Saturday week, when he won
the ex-students’ 150yds. in time given by two watches as
14 9-10 sec., and by a third as 15 l-ssec. The Association
Ground people ought to endeavour to bring about a meet-
ing between the crack amateur and one of the best pro-
fessionals in a series of races. It would be an interesting
affair, and there is “ millions in it.”

A policeman at Southport (Eng.) lately arrested
a cyclist for furious riding, and swore that the captured
wheelman was travelling at the rate of 40 miles an hour.
He would probably have sworn just as cheerfully that he
was doing 800 miles a minute if the case had required it.
A policeman swears with such tremendous velocity that
his ideas of speed get confused.

A Parliamentary return shows in N.S.W. that,
during the last twelve months, no fewer than 357,317
letters were registered addressed to owners or agents of
betting-houses, and during the same period 1,796,585
letters passed through the post addressed to owners of
recognised betting-houses. In the year 1890, the sum of
£431,500 was advertised in the Sydney papers in gambling,
sweeps, whilst in 1891 the amount had risen to £670,000.

* * #
A balance-sheet, lately issued by the “ Sowth ”
Melbourne Football Club, contains an awful mass of food
for reflection. The twenty wild untutored heroes of that
team cost £3O per head for a few days’ trip to Queensland.
They have consumed £325 in “ trophies ” during the
season, and been photographed all over the shop to the
tune of £133. This last is, perhaps, the saddest item of
them all. The fact that there are barrackers willing to
pay a shilling or two for a group photograph of the
glorious Sowth, can be defended on the’ground that the
shilling might be worse spent in getting drunk. But for
a secretary to scatter these souvenirs gratis over the fair
surface of Australia, as examples for the young, and in
the interests of society at large, is an appalling demon-
stration of football’s faith in itself. Another dishearten-
ing circumstance sticks out from this balance-sheet.
From a total club expenditure of £2462, the umpires—
the real heroes, who carry their lives in their hands, and
have to face sufficient blasphemy to kill a bullock—have
received ten pounds nineteen shillings. They haven’t
even the additional solace of being immortalised in the
£133 worth of photographs. This seems an oversight.
The umpire should always be specially grouped beside
the ball, as another object who does not know when to
expect the next kick.

The Tasmanian-bred horse Caledonian made a
show of his field in the Steeplechase at Randwick on
Saturday, as going to the front from the jump he was
never approached and won by over a furlong. He has
only one eye, but fenced faultlessly, and pulled up after
the race apparently as fresh as when he started.

Boyd Dunlop Morehead, in the Queens-
land Assembly, stated that “journalism was the
last refuge of a ruffian, except that provided by
Her Majesty at St. Helena ” (prison). Morehead
is not a ruffian. He is only a patriot.
We have received the first number of the
Australian Athlete, organ of the Sydney
Amateur Gymnastic Club. It is not fair to judge
any paper by its first number. If it were, we’d
conclude that the Australian Amateur Gymnastic
Club had a lot more muscle than brains.
Maoriland Legislative Council having
rejected the Government Labour Bill, and killed
its Land Bill, the Executive will flood that used-
up chamber with new appointments, and thus kill
the dog by running its tail through its scalp in-
stead of by choking it with butter.
N.S.W. Legislative Assembly has decided
not to deprive of votes the inmates of charitable
asylums. The nominee Legislative Council will,
of course, object to this, notwithstanding that
there are proportionately quite as many semi-
paralysed imbeciles in the Council as there are in
any charitable institution which is not purely and
absolutely an idiot-hospital. Anyone who doubts
this has only, after visiting the N.S.W. asylums
for lunatics, to go and have a look at the N.S.W.
asylum for Parkes’ dead-beat pals and benefactors.
“ They are my creatures,” saith Parkes.

They have merit.
The fact is indisputable.
The proof lies in the free samples.
It is not enough to say they sometimes fail.
They are of human produce, and therefore fallible.
The real facts are—
Do they cure diseases of the blood and skin in the majority of in-
stances? Undoubtedly they do.
Do they undo the effect of a chill or congestion, by bringing about
normal conditions of health ? Yes, they loosen and disperse the congestion.
Do they permeate the blood aseptically, so that bacilli and germs
cannot multiply and the growth of disease is checked ? Yes, they do.
Do they confer relief, assuage pain, and benefit the general health,
even when they do not cure ? Invariably, yes.
Do they act as a natural healthy laxative, rendering them especially
valuable to women and children, and to men of sedentary habits of life or
business 1 Yes, by tonic action only.
Do they free the inner skin pores and intricacies from taint, clearing
off eruptions and improving the complexion 1 They do.
Does their palatable taste and safety render them pre-eminently a
family medicine ? Yes, for neither the mind nor stomach rebels against
them, and children like them.
Are they the best of all blood purifiers and the most valuable of all
spring cleansing medicines ? Yes, they are.

Beaney’s biggest diamond ring was sold
for £l9l.
The wildly hilarious S. M. HfiRAkD draws
attention to a new device for preventing draughts
in railway carriages by “simply making the
inside widow lean to the outside at the top when
put up.” The spectacle of a widow—probably
with nine small children—leaning over at the top
when put up is too harrowing for comment. The
same paper also tells of a man who died leaving
U, window and family unprovided for, which is
another harrowing item, and calculated to draw
tears from a east-iron stove.
Blogging has been initiated in the Northern
Territory. The first to taste the public castigator
was, of course, a Chinaman. His crime was a
horrible one—an unnatural offence on a European
child five years old, whom he Was supposed to
nurse. Government Resident Knight, acting as
Judge, sent him up for five years and ordered 33
lashes along With it. Mothers in the Far North
are now seriously considering whether it isn’t
better to employ nurse-girls instead of Chinese
boys. Years ago another Chine e nurse was
proved to be a lep°r after he had been in his posi-
tion for several years.
Joseph Palmer, sharebroker. Foreign-
trader, boss Baptist, anti-gambling apostle, and
stock-exchange magnate, Writes to Sydney D.T.
about the unnaturally slow increase of population
in Canada, and finishes up thus ;
This is not surprising, hut is the natural result of two
great Calamities which have befallen Canada during the
fast id years. The calamities to which I refer are Pro-
tection and Federation.
Good old Foreign-trad 3 argument ! And the
decadence of Ireland, where the population,
instead of increasing slowly, has decreased 59 per
cent., is caused, We presume, by Freatrade and
the Want of Federation, that unhappy island
having been swallowed instead of federated. And
the pitiably slow progress of West Australia under
the old Imperial Foreign-trade regime, is owing,
we should imagine, to the same cause. And the
loss of half the population of the Scotch High-
lands comes, We should think, into the same cate-
gory. And, worst disaster of all, Joseph Palmer
himself seems to arise from Froetrale, and the
dismal sounds that issue from his head arc very
solemn things to listen to.
LITTLE OF THE GUN; NOTHING OF
THE DOG.
When Rip Van Winkle awoke from his nap in
the Gatskill Mountains in America, he found him-
self an old man. His dog was dead long ago, and
nothing was left of ins gun but the lock and
barrel. And when he went back to the village of
Falling Water, where he used to live, nobody
recognised him. His wife, believing him dead,
had married an innkeeper. Right enough too,
for Rip had lain on the mountain-side, sound
asleep, for twenty years.
A long snooze ; but it seems to me I’d rather be
asleep half my life at a stretch than to stop awake
and be miserable. Yet here is a woman who s iys,
“I can truthfully say that for eighteen years I
was never free from pain for a single day.”
Mercy! think of that. What a Wretchol Way
to live! Yet I suppose millions of folk crawl
along through the world in that style. Not be-
cause they want to. Heavens, no ! But because
they can’t help it. This was her situ ition, and
an army of other Women (besides crowds of men)
can sympathise with her.
She says : “For over twenty years I was weak
and sickly. At first I had a bad taste in the
mouth, poor appetite, and an uncomfortable feel-
ing at my chest and sides, and often tried to
obtain ease by holding my sides with my hands.
After eating I had great pain ; it was like a load
at my chest, and I could not bear it until I
vomited all my food up. I would be quite faint
from the want of food but was amid to cat. At
times I had bad attacks of spasms which nearly
doubled me up, and I rambled up and down the
house for hours together, for I could not even lie
down. Of course I lost a deal of sleep, and in a
morning was so weak and faint I scarcely knew
how to get cut of bed and downstairs. For
eighteen years I teas never free from pain for a
single day.
“ I saw doctor after do tor, and took a great
quantity of medicine without finding any real
relief. They would not say what was the cause of
my ailment. I was fast wasting a\> ly, and did
not think I could live much longer, wlo.n one day
in 1881 my husband heard from Mr. Joseph
Cooper, of Bourne, of a medicine called Mother
Seigel’s Curative Syrup, which he said had cured
him. ‘IfI am spared,’ I said to ray husband, ‘ I
will try it.’ I did so, and before I had taken the
first bottle I found relief, and after taking four
bottles more I was quite well and strong.
“ That is now ten years ago, and 1 have been in
good health ever since, taking just an occasional
dose of the syrup. After my recovery the clergy-
man said to my husband, “Your missis gets
about very different to what she used to do,’and
he told him that Mother Seigel’s Syrup had
wrought the cure. People tell me I look better
than I did twenty years ago, and I feel so strong
that I can now dig potatoes and do work with
anyone, notwithstanding I am 65 years of age.
“ My husband suffered terribly from rheuma-
tism and flux, and has found wonderful benefit
from the same medicine. He says he would have
been in the churchyard long ago but for Mother
Seigel’s Syrup. For myself, I can say it saved my
life, and I wish others to know what it has done
for me and mine. (Signed) Mrs. Esther Ayr,
wife of William Ayr, of Thurlby, near Bourne,
Lincolnshire.”
In this brief and simple way Mrs. Ayr tells a
story the full details of which would fill a book.
What hopes and fears ! what hours of keen sorrow
and deep darkness she must have experienced.
Yes, indeed. None can even imagine it except
those who belong to the great Sisterhood of Suffer-
ing. Where she mentions one symptom of her
disease she actually could have named a dozen.
For her malady—tiie fearfully common and fatal
one, indigestion and dyspepsia—has as many signs
and forms as the mind has fancies, or the heavens
have clouds. From it, as from sin, come a. thou-
sand pangs and pains to torment and to crush
helpless humanity.' Seeing what her great dis-
covery has accomplished in this and multitudes of
like eases, how good a friend to her kind was
Mother Seigel ! Rip Van Winkle awoke from his
nap to find himself old ; Mrs. Ayr awoke from her
long night of illness to find herself young again.
Is not the moral plain enough for all the suffering
millions in England?

Gov. Kintore on the Northern Territory.
Dear Bulletin,—Referring to your remarks re
Governor Kintore’s impudent assertion that
coloured labour is essential in the Northern
Territory of S.A., I think a short explanation
might not be out of place. Gov. Kintore wrote
(I quote from memory) that, if the Chinese were
taken away, the Palmerston (Port Darwin) resi-
dent would be left without servants, gardeners,
fishermen, laundries, tailors, etc. This statement,
while true in fact, is utterly misleading. The
general reader accepts Palmerston as the ordinary
Australian township with which we are all
familiar, whereas it is not, nor has it ever been,
anything of the sort. Palmerston, from the start,
was nothing more than a group of Government
officials living on the point of land that juts into
the harbour. There was, and is, a Government
Resident, living in a residency —a kind of
governor on a small scale ; a police-magistrate,
an inspector of police, and several troopers. The
inspector of the northern portion of the overland
line and his numerous staff reside there ; also the
representative of the Gable Company and his staff,
a chief surveyor and his staff, &c., &c. These
- formed for years the main elements of the place.
The Cable Co.’s officials, coming as they did from
Singapore and other eastern stations, and accus-
tomed to coloured service, went in for Chinese
cooks, &c., and, as a matter of course, the other
officials followed suit. The Chinese were cheap,
there were no white men to protest against their
employment, and a Chinese colony at once settled
down and monopolised whatever trade there was
going. The unfortunate premature rush to the
goldfields, which did the Territory so much harm,
was another attraction to the Chinese, who, on
the return of the disappointed miners, took up
the abandoned ground. The European store-
keepers and hotel-keepers, who settled in the
place as time went on, fell into the ways of the
town, and employed all Chinese servants for their
households. None but Chinese cooks were em-
ployed on the different stations on the overland
line. It will thus be seen that the formation of
Palmerston, and that of the many North Queens-
land towns in the same latitude, was, and is,
essentially different. The Queenslanders formed
their ports where needed, as convenient outlets for
pastoral and mining industries. What few
Government officials were considered necessary
followed the small nucleus of population, and did
not precede it. One police-magistrate did the
work for a far larger area than that under the
control of a resident, a P.M., and a police-
inspector in the Territory. The Queensland
lawns have grown into prosperous centres;
Palmerston, official and Chinese - ridden, has
lingered and languished. Governor Kintore
asserts that to banish the Chinese would deprive
the residents of domestic servants, gardeners,
&c.; and so it would until they got white servants
in their stead, and no longer. There are no white
servants in the place now simply because the
Chinaman leaves no room for them. The absurd
statement that Chinese labour is necessary to open
the country is palpably false. When Europeans
can work in Cairns, on Charters Towers, and on
the Etheridge they can work in the Territory.
Governor Kintore saw nothing of the Territory to
enable him to foim an independent, personal
judgment. He landed at Port Darwin, was feted
by the Chinese merchants who have sent thousands
out of the country, and are anxious to send thou-
sands more; he travelled down along the main
road that follows the telegraph-line, and was
posted up in partial statements by the
officials who form the local aristocracy. Of
the tableland country between the line and the
Georgina in Queensland, destined to be a great
sheep-raising district in the future, he saw no-
thing; of the country to the westward he saw
nothing. Ho simply saw the overland line—
Palmerston, and the little clique there, and it
cut him to the heart that they should be deprived
of their Chinese servants. As evincing any know-
ledge of the Territory, or its resources, the report
is a farce. As an official and authoritative state-
ment, which may lead to complications with the
English Government, who still retain a kind of
shadowy claim over the Territory, it is a dan-
gerous document. The Governor of South Aus-
tralia has gone beyond his province altogether.
He has made himself the mouthpiece and trumpet-
blower of prospective syndicates and land-boomers,
and in a matter that closely affects the whole of
Australia; for so long as the back door of Port
Darwin is open we may close all the front doors
against the Chinese in vain. In spite of Gov. Kin-
tore, if Palmerston has to rely for its future progress
upon the Chinese it will always remain what it is
now — a settlement of officials, and a Chinese
quarter vegetating amongst a lot of vacant allot-
ments owned by absentees. _

A Iderman MacGravv, of Goulburn (N. S. W.)
says he knows of a number of cases in Goulburn
in which cancer has been cut from one portion
of a bullock’s carcase and the rest of the carcase
sold for human consumption.
A rich American woman, in London, has
a splendid pearl necklace which, she boasts, has
never been off her neck, day or night, for four
years. Pearls, a local paper points out, are
improved by wearing, but are ruined by washing.
The Yankee woman’s pearls are still good.

pa, “bucks” at the idea of his hopeful heir not
marrying ready money, and so has forbidden Do
Moleyns’ banns with Miss Tranby Croft Wilson
despite her splendid prospects,

Government Statist Coghlan reports that
during the last agricultural season there were
altogether 8044 acres of grape vines inN.S. Wales.
3S9C acres, under vine cultivation, yielded 842,181
gallons of wine. All these latter vineyards, and
inore, will necessarily be torn up when Prohibition
comes along.
“A clergyman’s daughter” urges
homeopathic means be taken to cure the big-drum
disease of the Salvation Army. She suggests that
all the other sects turn out into the public streets
with shrieking sisterhoods and brass bands.
The N.S.W. Premier lately informed
Houghton, M.P., that if he will submit some
reliable and tangible information relative to the
registry-office scandals the Government will be
prepared to move in the matter. Some of the
required data could be obtained from the N.S.W.
Railway Department. Two years ago it was the
practice to issue free passes to the unemployed,
and one ingenious labour-agent used to procure
them for his victims to go to such far-distant
places as Tenterlield, Albury. and Bourke, in the
sure and certain hope that they would never
return. Of course he provided them with mythical
situations and took their half-sovereigns with a
sympathetic sigh.
George Francis Train lays it down as an
axiom that cranks are always moral, but if we
accept this ruling, then a large number of alleged
Australian cranks are put right out of court.
Train’s contention, however, is supported by any
amount of negative evidence. The methodical
profligate is generally “orthodox” in his politics,
and a cynical advocate of moderation. Your
money-making rogue is the antithesis of the crank,
and no first-class usurer ever had a bee in his bell-
topper. In inferior criminal society, too, the term
applied to any half-starving fellow who refuses to
mix with the “ boys ’’—is “crank.” And the fact
that the so-called musical or artistic crank is apt
to be loose in his morals, goes to prove that his
crankiness is merely a trick of the trade.
THE FOSTER BREWING COMPANY
LIMITED, MELBOURNE.
Whatever the great International Exhibition of
1888-9 may have cost the Victorian Government
it has undoubtedly proved a pronounced success
in introducing to Melbourne many protective
industries which, under ordinary circumstances
would not for some years at least have been
established. One of the most pronounced indus-
tries in this respect is the Foster Brewing Co.
Limited, situated at Collingwood, Melbourne, and
close to the Victoria Parade. The building
is built of red brick, two storeys in height,
and has a large frontage to Rokeby - street.
Such is the brewery in its present form. The old
building, however, was of much smaller dimen-
sions, but, nevertheless, the plant and effects were
sufficiently good to brew the celebrated Foster
Lager Beer which secured in competition with the
world the highest award at the Melbourne Cen-
tennial Exhibition of 1888-9. This successful issue
was brought about by Messrs. Foster Brothers
two enterprising Americans who afterwards
formed the business into the Foster Brewing Co.
Limited, and with what success we will endeavour
to describe. Their first step was to erect the
building at present in use, and to secure the best
and latest improved machinery adapted to brew-
ing lager beer as a speciality. In brewing lager
beer it is well known that the process is reversed
as compared witli the usual method adopted in the
manufacture of Australian beer or English ale.
The “ Lager” can only be produced by preserving
a low temperature, and in order to obtain this
some very special machinery is used by the Foster
Co. This is first noticeable in a 60 h.p. bi-valve
engine for freezing purposes, and connected with
a 12-ton ice-machine. This engine bears the
manufacturing name, J. S. Schwalbk and Solm,
Germania, and in the machinery court was one of
the features of the Centennial Exhibition. There
is a second engine 45 h.p., by the Pictet Ice Co.
of New York, to which another ice-machine is
attached. These two important factors supply all
the refrigerating requirements in connection with
the brewery. In consequence of having to
“ refrigerate ” the principal rooms of the building
it combines a twofold advantage, serving, its pur-
pose in reducing the temperature for brewing and
also producing ice which is sold throughout Mel-
bourne as a marketable commodity. The ice-
tanks are contained in the refrigerating rooms, and
within the tanks are square galvanised iron cells
each capable of holding of ice. These cells
are built in sections which are lifted out by means
of a small steam crane, and by applying warm
water the ice-blocks are “thawed” out of the
cells which are refilled by means of an elevated
syphon fixed on a roller bar. In another
room, still on a par with the temperature
of the Arctic regions—six freezing tanks are
in use for the manufacture of crystal ice,
whicli is considered to be a better article
than the ordinary ice block. The “Crystal”
block is the outcome of a freezing process much
slower than usual, but in 48 hours is perfected in
J-cwt. mould blocks. Next to the various freezing
rooms we visit the large ice store, capable of
storing 80 to ICO tons, which is kept in its beauti-
ful state of “coldness” owing to a crystalized
“ brine,” which is driven into the ice store from
the refrigerating room by means of a Blake pump.
There are nine of these Blake pumps used on the
premises. The main cellar, where the bulk lager
beer is stored, is kept at freezing point by means
of the salt water brine, which assumes a crystaline
form, the same as that used in the ice store and
most other portions of the building.
The brewing department comes next. One of
the principal features of the brewing is the
“mash,” and here Mr. Peska explained the
different degrees of temperature as between the
lager brew and that of ordinary colonial ales.
Colonial ale while in the “mash” begins at a
temperature of 175 deg. Fab. and finishes at ICO to
162 Fall., while the lager in process of “mash”
starts at 50 to 60 deg. Fah., and finishes at from
160 to 162 deg. Fah. Thus it is that the soluble
albuminous matter can only le obtained at a
very low temperature and increases very slowly,
while at a temperature of 160 to 162 deg. all
starch and extraneous substances are extracted.
Only the best brewing material is used by the
Foster Brewing Co. Bohemian hops of the
first quality and specially-prepared Australian
malt of a very pale colour. The large steam
copper boiler is another feature of the establish-
ment. It is built on the principle adopted by the
lager beer breweries of Copenhagen, which in point
of completeness excel those of Germany and
America. The top floor of the brewery is used as
a cooling-room and contains an enormous shallow
iron tank for exposing the beer in order that it
may contract a certain quantity of oxygen from
the air to further aid good fermentation, and so
that all insoluble matter may fall to the bottom
and thereby leave as near as possible a nice bright,
brilliant “warb.” The liquor then runs from
the cooler to the refrigerating tubes filled with
iced-water for the purpose of reducing the
high temperature to a degree for fermentation.
Probably the most interesting feature of this large
establishment is the special cultivation of abso-
lutely pure yeast by one of Dr. Hansen’s patent
machines of Copenhagen. The germ is imported
perfectly pure in sterile bottles and in most
minute particles; it is specially selected for
tropical climates, is absolutely proof against bac-
teria and deleterious matter, and is made m a
Melbourne laboratory specially for the roster
Brewing Co., Limited. The fermenting-room is
exceptionally large. It contains 14 vats, each ya
holding one brew of 80 hhds. The fermentation
takes place very slowly, and the yeast sinks o
the bottom ; in the ordinary course of brewing
this is reversed, the yeast rising to the top. J n
second fermentation takes place in the cel a ,
which has great storage capacity. It conuu
numerous large casks capable of holding /00 to
gals, each ; from these casks the liquor is pumpe
through a patent filter into the boiling-room, an
is there finished off for commercial uses. ■*-
premises fairly bristle with cleanliness—a gr®
secret in the management of an imports
brewery. All the floors are washed . oufc , a hv
scrubbed weekly. The export trade enjoyed
the Foster Brewing Co., Limited, is very la g
throughout Austialasia, and their a^er , /,
acknowledged to be the best value in the mark >
it has gained a meritorious mention wherever it
been tried, besides the price is so reasonable a
come within reach of most ordinary beer-drink
The managing directors are Mr. A.• D. n
(chairman), Mr. C. G. Turner and Mr. Lames
Thompson, who, with the general boar
directors, comprise some of the smartest me
the city of Melbourne. The officers ot ,
Brewery are Mr. R. Henry Talbot, g| n „
manager; Mr. Joseph Peska, brewer; ‘
Bohnki, refrigerating engineer, whilst Mr.
Martin (formerly of Toohey and Co., Sydney) j.
charge of the bottling-department. Ihe P r • . 0
output of Foster Brewing Co. is so large tha
works are kept continually going.

To H. F. Kuqelmann, Esq.
Dear Sir,—This is to certify that I, Patrick Keenan, was
taken ill about three years ago with a severe cold, which
turned into Bronchitis and became chronic. I consulted
more than one doctor, and during that lime took a great
quantity of medicine without it doing me the slightest,
good, but I became worse every day, wasting away' until
J became a perfect skeleton. About February last I began
to spit up blood, and what with the cough and severe
pains in my left lung, and the quantity of bad matter I
was expectorating every day, I became very much alarmed.
Hearing of your professional skill I consulted you, and
you told me you could cure me for a given sum. I agreed
and went under your treatment in March last. At the
end of three months I was taken bad one morning, and
romited about two pints of blood, matter and stringy
phlegm. From that time I commenced to improve, and
was gaining in weight every day. About a month ago the
blood ceased coming, the cough disappeared, and the
lung healed completely, and thank God and your pro-
fessional skill, I am as well as ever.
P. KEENAN.
Declared and subscribed before me, one of Her Majesty’s
Justices of the Peace, for the Southern Bailiwick, in the
County of Grant, this 10th day of December, 1888.
Peter Smytiie, J.P.
